# CSS Essential Training (2023)

> Cascading Style Sheets (CSS) is a stylesheet language that allows you to control the appearance of your webpages. In this hands-on course, instructor Christina Truong demonstrates the concepts that form the foundation of CSS, explaining what you need to know to tweak existing CSS and start writing your own with ease. Christina explains how to add colors and other design elements to take your webpa

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023) | 5h 26m | Beginner | 23K learners

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/getting-started-with-css?u=76281980&t=0)** - CSS is a fundamental part of [[Web Design]] because it controls how your webpages look.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/getting-started-with-css?u=76281980&t=5)** Without it, websites would only display text on white backgrounds.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/getting-started-with-css?u=76281980&t=10)** Use CSS to take your plain [[HTML]] websites to the next level.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/getting-started-with-css?u=76281980&t=14)** Whether you're just trying to customize your Squarespace page or contemplating a career switch, learning the essentials of CSS from the ground up gives you the ability to do everything from tweaking existing CSS to writing your own.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/getting-started-with-css?u=76281980&t=27)** By the end of this course, you'll have an actual project.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/getting-started-with-css?u=76281980&t=30)** An online portfolio and resume page.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/getting-started-with-css?u=76281980&t=33)** I'm Christina Truong, and I invite you to join me from my course on [[LinkedIn]] Learning where I'll cover the foundations of CSS.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/getting-started-with-css?u=76281980&t=41)** Let's get started.

### 1. Getting Started

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [HTML and CSS](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/html-and-css?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/html-and-css?u=76281980&t=0)** - In this course we'll be focusing on the fundamentals of CSS, but we'll also have discussions about [[HTML]] because these two languages work closely together.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/html-and-css?u=76281980&t=9)** HTML was originally created for sharing research documents.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/html-and-css?u=76281980&t=13)** So the space between paragraphs and bold headings are examples of default browser styles which were created to make text easier to read.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/html-and-css?u=76281980&t=21)** Though HTML elements are displayed with these basic styles, avoid choosing a particular element because of the way it looks.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/html-and-css?u=76281980&t=28)** That's what CSS is for.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/html-and-css?u=76281980&t=30)** Instead use HTML to define the structure of the document and add meaning to the content.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/html-and-css?u=76281980&t=36)** CSS, on the other hand, controls the appearance of the document and separates presentation from the content.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/html-and-css?u=76281980&t=42)** Throughout this course, we'll be working with HTML, but it's not the focus.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/html-and-css?u=76281980&t=46)** And as you're following along, if you have any questions about HTML that we're not covering, I would encourage you to check out [[HTML Essential Training]] for more details.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/html-and-css?u=76281980&t=56)** We'll also be building out a real world project, a personal website.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/html-and-css?u=76281980&t=60)** So before getting into the essentials of CSS, we'll start with an overview of how to set up a development environment and prepare our course project files.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[HTML]] (7)
> **Env Vars:** html (7), css (4)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)

#### [Working with website files](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=0)** - The web started out as digital copies of text-based research documents, but has since grown into a lot more and can be viewed on a variety of devices.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=9)** Websites can be designed as a single page with content divided into sections within the same [[HTML]] page.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=16)** For multi-page websites related content is spread out over multiple HTML pages.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=21)** These webpage are written using coding languages such as HTML and CSS, and contain other web resources such as images.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=30)** When creating a website, you're basically creating a collection of files which are embedded or linked to each other.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=36)** These different types of files are saved with specific file extensions.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=40)** For example, HTML is contained within .HTML files, CSS in .CSS files.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=47)** Other web resources like images, videos, or PDFs also have their own file extensions.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=53)** Developing a website from scratch usually starts with creating and working on these files locally, on your computer.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=60)** That way, you can make changes and test them in your own workspace before making your website available to the public.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=67)** These files are stored in various folders, also referred to as directories.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=72)** The main folder is referred to as the root directory, which is where HTML files are generally stored.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=79)** Other files are usually grouped into related sub folders.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=84)** Linking to files is an important part of building a website, so it's equally important to follow a consistent structure.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=90)** Here are some conventions that can be followed when naming files.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=94)** Spaces and symbols should be avoided, with the exception of underscores and hyphens, which can be used to separate words.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=101)** Just note that [[Google]]'s [[Search Engine Optimization (SEO)|Search Engine Optimization]] Guide recommends separating words with hyphens.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=107)** Some servers will also interpret different letter casing as different names, so skip the unnecessary headaches and keep it consistent by using all lowercase letters.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=117)** Also, using meaningful and descriptive names is recommended for SEO, and it also just makes it easier for us to identify which file is used for what.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=127)** When you're ready to go live with your website, all the files contained within the root folder are uploaded to a web server, so these naming conventions don't apply to the root folder, but you might as well get into the habit of keeping it consistent for the entire project.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=141)** To make a website live, you'll also need a domain name, which is the address of the website.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=147)** The server stores the web files, and the web-hosting companies provide access to these servers.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=154)** However, if you use a browser-based text editor such as Glitch, these tools are already online and live.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=161)** Each project is automatically assigned a unique URL, which anyone can view.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=166)** We'll also be using a another online editor, [[CodePen]], for smaller exercises.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=172)** This will allow us to focus on individual concepts.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=175)** Online text editors are ideal for experimental projects and working on small code snippets.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=181)** For the course project, however, we'll be using a text editor to work on our files locally to build out our personal websites.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=187)** This process mirrors the typical workflow of a web developer, giving you a more realistic experience.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/working-with-website-files?u=76281980&t=194)** In this course, we won't be making our websites live, but if you're interested in learning about the different ways to publish a website, check out my course, "Getting Your Website Online."

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/customizing-your-text-editor?u=76281980&t=0)** - We've covered the basics of using a text editor, opening folders and files and making changes to the code.

> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/customizing-your-text-editor?u=76281980&t=6)** However, the purpose of using an advanced text editor is to access more features and customize the settings to your liking.

> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/customizing-your-text-editor?u=76281980&t=26)** Let's start with updating the font size settings.
>

>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/customizing-your-text-editor?u=76281980&t=35)** This will make everything bigger or smaller including the UI elements like the icons and texts in the sidebar.

>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/customizing-your-text-editor?u=76281980&t=89)** Bounded will wrap to the width of the editor window or the wordWrapColumn width, whichever is smaller.

#### [Where to find images](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Using your own photos is a great option, but it's not always possible to create high-quality graphics on your own.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=6)** So let's talk about where to find images for your website.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=10)** A popular option is to use stock photography, which are a collection of photos typically taken by professional photographers that can be licensed for specific uses.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=21)** For navigation elements and social media links, icons can also be used to add visual interest.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=27)** There are also paid services such as Adobe Stock, which gives you access to photos, as well as illustrations, videos, templates, and more.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=36)** But if you're looking for a simple and free option, Unsplash is pretty good too.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=41)** For icons, Iconfinder has filters for free and premium icons, styles, and licenses.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=48)** Just do a search for what you're looking for to see all the options.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=53)** Speaking of licenses, even if an image or icon is free, make sure to check for any copyright, usage, or attribution requirements.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=61)** This information is usually included on the website or with the download in a text file.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=68)** When using icons, you'll often find branded company logos.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=73)** For example, let's do a search for the Twitter logo.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=81)** We can see different variations for the logo, but many organizations actually have specific usage restrictions.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=89)** Here are the guidelines for the Twitter logo.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=94)** If we scroll through the document, we'll see guidelines for how to use the Twitter logo, specifically that it must not be changed in any way or use any out of date versions.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=106)** Truthfully, I'm not sure what will happen if you're caught violating these rules but most likely you'll be asked to remove the content.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=112)** So just to be safe, do your best to follow the guidelines.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=117)** As we've seen in the last exercise, I've already downloaded the icons, but most social media platforms have their own specifications, so if you'd like to learn more, I've included links to these guides in the links PDF file contained in the exercise files.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=135)** Another option is to use online placeholder image services like Loren Picsum.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=140)** They offer different options for choosing photos, dimensions, and styles by specific URLs listed in their documentation.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=150)** It's important to note that if this service goes down, that images will no longer be available.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=155)** Placeholder images can be useful, but they're meant to be temporary, so use them for testing or for examples.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/where-to-find-images?u=76281980&t=162)** It's best to have as much content ready as possible before building your website, but when that isn't possible, try to use realistic placeholder content.

#### [Optimizing images for the web](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=0)** - In the early days of the internet, waiting for an image to load was just the way it was, but these days, users want it to feel instant.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=8)** To improve website speed, resizing image files will make them smaller in size.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=13)** Most photos have dimensions of 4,000 by 3000 pixels or larger, which is used for print, but for the web, it's way too big.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=22)** For faster load times, files should be as small as possible, especially for mobile devices, which may have data usage or internet speed restrictions.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=31)** But what size should we use?
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=33)** Well, that depends on the amount of space it will take up on the page.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=38)** In the course project, we'll be using a background image to cover the entire homepage and the header portion of the resume page.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=45)** In the project section, the thumbnails will be set to 300 pixels wide for larger screens, and 500 pixels wide for smaller screens.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=54)** But we can't just resize the image files to these dimensions because it also depends on the type of screen the website is being displayed on.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=63)** Different screens have varying pixel densities, which refer to the number of pixels in a physical area on a screen.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=70)** This is measured by pixels per inch, or PPI for short.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=74)** Higher pixel densities result in sharper and more detailed images and text.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=79)** For example, Apple's Retina displays have four times as many pixels per inch as non-Retina displays because they can fit two pixels within the same width and height.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=91)** The term Retina is a trademark of Apple but it's often used to refer to high-density screens in general.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=97)** To support screens with different pixel densities, use one image file for standard screens and another image file that's at least twice the size for screens with higher pixel densities.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=108)** [[HTML]] can then be used to deliver the correct image file using the source attribute for the standard size image and the source set attribute for the larger image.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=117)** The 2x descriptor indicates that the larger image is for displays with a 2x resolution.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=124)** For the course project thumbnails, we can use the same image for both desktop and mobile screens.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=129)** A 300-pixel versus a 500-pixel wide image won't significantly change the file size.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=136)** We'll use the larger of the two, because using a smaller image file and styling it to be larger can pixelate the image.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=143)** So for standard screens, the image will be 500 pixels wide, and for high-pixel density screens, it will be 1000 pixels wide.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=151)** Then, we'll use CSS to adjust the widths for the desktop and mobile layouts.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=157)** For the background images, sizing the file to be twice the size of an entire computer screen would be quite large.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=164)** For this scenario, instead of creating two image files, using one that's 1500 pixels to 2500 pixels wide is usually big enough.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=174)** The placeholder images included in the exercise files are already sized accordingly.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=181)** If you're using your own images or want to find different stock photography options, you'll need to use image editing software like Photoshop to resize the files.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=189)** There are also free online tools like Adobe Express and Shutterstock, which are used just for resizing images.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=197)** When you resize the width of an image, the height is automatically adjusted as well to maintain the aspect ratio.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=204)** You may need to adjust the height later, depending on the image you chose.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=207)** It will become more apparent when we start adding CSS to our projects.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/optimizing-images-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=211)** Just a reminder, all these links are included in the Links.pdf file, as well as some additional resources about image files and optimization techniques.

#### [Relative and absolute paths](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When building websites, it's common to link different files together, such as embedding an image, linking to a CSS file, or even another website.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=9)** This can be done using a relative or absolute path.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=12)** Relative paths are used to link to files within the same project.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=16)** The path is determined by the file's location within the directory.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=21)** In the course project, the link to the resume page from the homepage uses a relative path.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=28)** Since the [[HTML]] files are in the same folder, the file path is just the file name.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=35)** To link to a file contained in a folder, start the file path with the folder name followed by a slash and the file name.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=43)** These paths are relative to their location in the directory, so if you move a file, you'll need to update the paths accordingly.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=51)** File paths are also case sensitive, so if you have a folder named css with lowercase letters, and you reference it with uppercase letters, the link may not work.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=60)** To avoid potential issues, it's recommended to use all lowercase letters.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=66)** Absolute paths are typically used for linking to external web resources outside of your project files.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=72)** This method uses the full URL, including the protocol, domain name, and any directory or file names.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=80)** Linking to a website or a specific webpage is usually okay because when someone follows that link, they're directed to another website.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=88)** However, embedding a web resource, such as an image from one website into your own website, should be avoided.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=97)** This is called hotlinking.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=99)** If you are linking to a file on someone else's server, when a user visits your page, their browser will request the image from the original website server.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=110)** This can consume the bandwidth of the hosting service that the website owner paid for and potentially cause the server to slow down or crash.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=118)** Hotlinking is basically using resources without permission, unless, of course, you do have permission.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=125)** There are services that are designed for hosting resources for others to use, such as the placeholder image service I mentioned in a previous lesson, Lorem Picsum.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=134)** When using a service like this, an absolute path must be used because these images are hosted on a live website.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=142)** Most placeholder image services will offer various options that will change the URL.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=149)** Just make sure to copy the whole path into your project.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=153)** Also, keep in mind that these services are meant to be temporary.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/relative-and-absolute-paths?u=76281980&t=157)** When you're ready to launch a website, it's better to use your own images or license stock photography.

> **CLI Commands:** make (1)

#### [Referencing CSS](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Before we get into writing CSS, let's discuss three ways to add CSS to the [[HTML]]: inline, internal, and external.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=9)** The inline method uses a style attribute added to the opening HTML tag.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=16)** The CSS style rules are added as the value of the attribute.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=21)** Since the styles are applied directly to each element, they are not reusable.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=26)** Each style declaration must be added to each element, even if it's the same style.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=32)** Also, multiple styles must be added to the same style attribute which can make it difficult to read and manage.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=40)** Inline styles will override other CSS style rules added by other methods, potentially leading to conflicts.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=47)** They should be used sparingly if at all since this method is not flexible and can be difficult to maintain.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=54)** With the internal method, the CSS is added to the head section of the HTML document.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=60)** All the CSS style rules are enclosed between style tags.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=65)** CSS selectors can then be used to apply styles to all the matched elements instead of each element individually.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=72)** In this example, p is the selector and will apply the style to all paragraph elements.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=78)** To apply the same style to multiple HTML pages, the style block must be added to each page.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=85)** So while this method is more flexible than inline CSS, it has its drawbacks too.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=91)** Most websites require much more CSS than in the example, so this method can also become hard to maintain.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=98)** The third option is the external method, which is the recommended option.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=102)** This involves using a separate file for the CSS which is referenced in the HTML file using the link tag.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=110)** This is a void HTML element so it doesn't need a closing tag.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=116)** It's also included in the head section of the document.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=120)** To link to the CSS file, include the rel attribute with the value of stylesheet, and the href attribute value is the path to the CSS file.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=130)** An external stylesheet can be shared among multiple HTML files in the project by linking to the same CSS file.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=138)** Any changes made to the stylesheet will be applied to all the HTML pages it's linked to.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=143)** Of the three methods, using an external stylesheet is recommended because it separates the CSS from the HTML.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=150)** It can be reused and it's easier to manage.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=153)** As we move through this course, you'll see that there are often different ways to accomplish the same thing.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/referencing-css?u=76281980&t=158)** I'll be sure to go over best practices to help you decide which option works best for each scenario.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[HTML]] (9)
> **Env Vars:** css (14), html (9)
> **Best Practices:** recommended (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [CSS specifications and the W3C](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=0)** - When writing any coding language, there are specific rules to follow.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=4)** For CSS, the W3C is the organization that creates the technical specifications.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=10)** When I started learning CSS, I was determined to read the whole thing.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=15)** However, I found that reading the W3C guidelines was a little intimidating.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=20)** There was a lot of unfamiliar jargon, and it was quite extensive.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=23)** So needless to say, I didn't finish it.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=26)** But just like any other reference manual, you don't need to read it [[Microsoft Word|word]] for word.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=30)** You just need to know how to find what you're looking for, and how to read the documentation.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=35)** Since the W3C is responsible for establishing these rules, you'll probably end up looking at it at some point.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=41)** So let's go over the publishing process to better understand where CSS specifications come from, and how to keep up to date with the changes.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=50)** All of the past, current, and working drafts are published on the W3C website.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=60)** The first two versions, CSS level 1 and CSS level 2, were published as one whole document.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=66)** After level 2, the specification was split into modules, so each module could be updated independently, which allowed for faster updates.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=75)** For example, we can see the full CSS level 2 specs right here.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=81)** And for just selectors, we can see it's currently at level 3.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=86)** The term CSS3 refers to any updates after CSS level 2, but since the specs are now split into modules, levels no longer refer to versions of CSS as a whole.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=99)** The levels are just versions for that particular module, so there's no need to make a distinction.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=105)** It's all just CSS.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=107)** When you select a module, you'll get all the information about that particular CSS concept, like what it's used for and how to use it.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=120)** There are also colors and status codes used to show what phase the particular module is at.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=128)** Here's an explanation for the status codes.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=131)** Features in the draft stages are experimental, and may not be supported by all browsers.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=136)** They can also be changed or removed.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=138)** Candidate or proposed recommendations become formalized after feedback and approval.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=144)** Standards at this phase are usually okay to start implementing.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=148)** The current standard for the latest browsers is recommendation, while superseded recommendation is a spec that has been replaced by a newer version.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=156)** You don't need to read the entire specification to write CSS, but it's good to have some understanding of the process.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=164)** In this course, I'll cover the essentials of CSS, but learning everything there is to know about CSS is more than what we can do in a few hours.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=172)** So continuous learning is an important part of the process.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=176)** It's also good to have some go-to resources, like the Mozilla Developer Network's CSS Guide, which is a friendlier read, and it links back to the W3C guides if you need more information.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-specifications-and-the-w3c?u=76281980&t=186)** Now that we know where the CSS specs come from, let's get into the core concepts.

#### [CSS syntax and terminology](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=0)** - Learning to code is similar to learning a spoken language.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=3)** You start by learning the grammar rules.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=5)** In coding, syntax is like the grammar rules of the coding language, and using proper syntax is how we give instructions to the computer.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=13)** Let's take a look at the CSS we added to our last project exercise and review the syntax in terminology.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=21)** This is a declaration block.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=23)** It may contain one or more style rules enclosed in curly brackets.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=28)** A selector specifies which [[HTML]] element or elements to apply the style to.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=34)** CSS style rules are called declarations and use property value pairs to provide instructions to the browser on how to style a specific HTML element.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=45)** The property specifies what is being styled like background.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=49)** The value defines the specific characteristics such as the color pale turquoise.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=55)** They are separated by a colon and end with a semicolon to indicate the end of the rule.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=60)** There are many CSS properties and values, and they all have different effects on the appearance and behavior of HTML elements.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=68)** We'll go over different properties and values throughout the course.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=72)** Some CSS styles can be defined using shorthand properties, which set multiple styles in one statement.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=79)** Longhand properties set values individually.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=83)** For example, padding is shorthand for padding top, padding right, padding bottom, and padding left.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=90)** Shorthand is generally more efficient since only one declaration is needed, but longhand properties can be used if you need to define just one characteristic.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=100)** Comments are used to add notes to your CSS.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=103)** They don't affect the styling of the webpage and are ignored by browsers, so we can use them to help us read our code better.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=111)** The syntax starts with a slash and asterisk and ends in the opposite order.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=116)** Write your comment using text, numbers or any type of characters as long as they are between the asterisks.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=124)** For example, notes, instructions, or extra information can be left for yourself or for your team.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=130)** In this example, the comment describes where the styles are applied, the header area, and what color the hex code represents.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=139)** To debug or experiment without deleting the CSS, you can temporarily hide it by commenting out a line or a whole block.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=147)** Just add the slash and asterisk before and after the section or line you want to hide.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=153)** White space in CSS is used in a couple different ways.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=157)** Sometimes it's required syntax.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=159)** For example, multiple property values must be separated by a space.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=164)** Without it, the value will not be valid and the styles will not be applied.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=169)** Other times it's used to format the code.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=172)** If we wrote the CSS with only the required white space, the browser would still be able to read it.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=178)** But writing CSS like this would be difficult for us to read.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=182)** It's common to write each declaration on a separate line and to indent them to easily see which block they're contained in.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=190)** Adding spaces between selector names and the curly brackets and between property value pairs can also improve readability.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=198)** As we go through different CSS concepts, I'll be sure to mention when white space is required in the syntax.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=204)** This is also something to pay attention to when reading documentation.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=209)** When it comes to formatting, developers tend to have strong opinions, including myself.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=214)** There have been countless debates and style guides written about this, such as this one: [codeguide.co](https://codeguide.co).
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=222)** Some of these rules may not make sense right now since we've only just started talking about CSS, but you can save this guide for later.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=231)** As you write more code, you'll develop your own style.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=233)** My advice is to do what makes sense to you but try to be consistent.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-syntax-and-terminology?u=76281980&t=237)** And if you're working on a team, I would recommend defining a cohesive style guide together.

#### [CSS values and units](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] In CSS, the property specifies the style to be changed, like color or font size.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=6)** The value defines that style, and is specific to the property, like red or 10 pixels.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=13)** Each property can be defined by different types of values, which can also be used with different units.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=20)** This lesson will be an overview of some commonly used value types and units.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=24)** However, how to use specific properties and values will be covered in other lessons throughout the course.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=31)** In CSS documentation, value types, also referred to as data types, are usually written with angled brackets to distinguish them from properties.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=41)** Although angled brackets are also used with [[HTML]], these value types are not HTML, this is just the writing convention for the specifications.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=51)** Let's start with an overview of numeric data types.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=55)** This category refers to data types that use numbers with or without units, to define styles such as the width and height of an element, the thickness of a border, or the number of times an animation should run.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=68)** An integer data type is a whole number that can be negative or positive.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=73)** A number data type can represent a whole number, or a fraction with decimals.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=79)** A percentage data type is a fraction of another value, usually a parent or ancestor element.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=85)** It can be a whole number or decimal value.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=88)** A dimension is basically a number value with a unit.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=92)** It represents other data types, such as time to specify durations, resolutions, and length, which we'll be using a lot throughout this course.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=102)** The length data type is used to define a distance value, such as the width of an element, or the size of text on a page.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=111)** The value is a number, and must be used with one of two types of units, absolute or relative.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=118)** Absolute length units are fixed, and based on a physical measurement.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=123)** Pixels are used for [[Web Development]], since screens are measured in pixels The other units are typically used for print designs.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=130)** Relative units define a length based on another length.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=134)** For example, em is relative to the font size of an ancestor element, rem is relative to the root element, the HTML element.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=144)** Viewport-based units are relative to the visible portion of the browser window.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=149)** Vw represents the width of the viewport, vh represents the height of the viewport, vmin and vmax are based on the smaller and larger dimensions of the viewport, respectively.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=162)** A key [[Microsoft Word|word]] is a textual data type, we used one in our first CSS exercise to set the background color of the webpage.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=170)** Keywords are predefined values, and will vary based on the property.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=175)** All valid keywords for each property are documented in various CSS specifications, such as the W3C publications and the MDN Web Docs.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=185)** Function values represent a more complex data type.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=189)** You can recognize a function by its syntax, the function name followed by parentheses.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=194)** Functions include one or more arguments, which are used to compute the resulting value.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=200)** There are many ways to define CSS properties, but some are more frequently used than others.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/css-values-and-units?u=76281980&t=205)** If you'd like to learn more, check out this MDN article for a comprehensive look at CSS values and units.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's talk about the color property which is used to change an element's text color and decoration which are styles applied to the text like an underline or strikethrough.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=10)** The color property is used with different color values.
>

>

>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=28)** Such as mediumseagreen or oldlace.

>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=57)** Plus it lists other types of color values, the RGB, HEX value and functional values that correspond with the keyword.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=64)** Just note that there are less than 150 keyword values, so for more color options, use one of the other value types instead.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=72)** One option is an RGB color value expressed with the RGB function.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=77)** The syntax uses three numbers to represent the red, green and blue channels.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=82)** Separated by spaces.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=84)** Each number is arranged from zero to 255.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=88)** Zero equals black and 255 equals white.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=92)** To add transparency, an optional fourth value can be added following a slash and uses a number between zero and one or a percentage between zero and 100.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=103)** In previous specifications, the numbers were separated by commas.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=107)** Also, to add transparency, an alpha channel was added using an RGBA function.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=114)** This is referred to as legacy syntax and it's still supported by browsers.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=118)** So if you've used it in previous projects, you should be fine.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=122)** But for new projects, use the current space-separated syntax instead.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=127)** Another type of RGB color value is the hexadecimal value.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=131)** This value is defined with six characters, using a combination of numbers from zero to nine and letters from A to F.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=139)** The first two characters represents the red value, the next two represents green and the last two represent blue.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=146)** The hex value must start with a number sign and is also case insensitive.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=151)** Hex values can be abbreviated if all the characters are the same, or if the pairs are the same.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=157)** For example, six 0s are the same as three 0s and F00 is the same as FF0000.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=166)** An alpha channel can also be added to the hex notation with one or two additional characters.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=172)** Zero is fully transparent and FF is fully opaque.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=178)** To find RGB values, you can use software such as Photoshop or Illustrator.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=182)** There are also various online tools which I will discuss in an upcoming lesson but how do you figure out what value to use for setting the alpha channel if zero is transparent and FF is fully opaque.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=195)** These numbers use a base-16 hexadecimal number system.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=199)** 16 different symbols are used to denote the values from zero to 15.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=204)** Zero to nine equals zero to nine.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=207)** But 10 to 15 is represented by the letters A through F.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=212)** So unlike the RGB functional color value which uses a percentage or a base-10 decimal value, setting the alpha channel for hex values also uses this base-16 system.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-color-property-and-values?u=76281980&t=225)** If you're not familiar with the base-16 system, no worries.
>

#### [Type and universal selectors](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In CSS, selectors are used to select specific [[HTML]] elements and apply styles to them.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=7)** Different types of selectors are used to determine which element the style should be applied to.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=12)** Let's start by taking a look at how to use two basic selectors, type and universal.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=18)** Type selectors are sometimes called an element selector, and they match to HTML elements by using the element name but without the angled brackets.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=27)** For example, to apply a style to all the h2 elements on your webpage, use h2 as the selector.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=35)** The universal selector can be used to select every single element in the whole document.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=43)** The syntax is represented by a single asterisk.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=47)** Using the universal selector can impact performance since it applies the styles to all elements on the page which is why it's not used very often.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=56)** There are some special use cases which we'll discuss in more detail later in the course.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=62)** Let's practice using selectors with a [[CodePen]] exercise.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=66)** The default layout of the CodePen editor shows three panels at the top of the page, but since we'll only be writing HTML and CSS, I'll minimize the [[JavaScript]] panel.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=78)** To maximize the space, I also like to put the panels on the side.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=82)** To change the editor view, select the Change View button in the menu bar.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=86)** The icon looks like a bunch of squares.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=89)** And the panels can be moved to the left or right.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=93)** I'll choose left, but you can, of course, choose whichever layout you prefer.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=97)** Let's start with adding a universal selector which uses the asterisk symbol.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=105)** Then add the left curly bracket.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=108)** In CodePen, the right curly bracket will automatically be added.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=112)** Press Enter to move the cursor between the brackets which also automatically adds the indentation.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=118)** Then we'll add the color property followed by a colon and any color value.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=124)** I'll go with purple and end it with a semicolon.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=128)** In CodePen, the styles are updated automatically.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=131)** You don't have to refresh the browser.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=133)** Now we can see in the output panel the color style has been applied to all the elements.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=138)** Now let's change the color of just the h1 heading.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=142)** What selector should it be?
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=145)** If you thought h1, you would be correct.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=148)** This is an example of the type selector.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=151)** On a new line, type in just h1 followed by the curly brackets and Enter to add your style declaration, we'll use color again.

>

>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=168)** Now the style is only applied to the h1 since that's the only element this selector matches to.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=174)** If we change it to h2, then the styles will be applied to both h2 headings.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=181)** Type selectors are often used to apply style to all of the matched elements, but what if you want to be more specific and add a style to only one particular element?
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/type-and-universal-selectors?u=76281980&t=191)** There are other selectors for this purpose which we'll discuss in the next lesson.

#### [ID selectors](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Unlike a type or universal selector, which can match to multiple elements on a page, an ID selector matches to a single unique element.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=9)** To use an ID selector, an ID attribute must first be added to the elements opening tag.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=15)** ID equals a value in quotes.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=18)** The value is defined by you.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=20)** The value is then used as the CSS selector starting with a number sign.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=25)** When choosing an ID name, don't include any spaces.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=29)** If you want to separate multiple words, use in underscore or dash.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=34)** Camel case where each new [[Microsoft Word|word]] is capitalized is also valid.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=37)** But using lowercase letters is a more common writing style in CSS.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=42)** Also using descriptive names that explain the purpose of the style can make it easier to read, and understand why it's being used.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=50)** For example, an ID name like Global Header implies that the styles are for a header used throughout the website.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=58)** Let's open another [[CodePen]] example.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=61)** To add a style to just the first paragraph, let's add an ID attribute to the opening tag.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=69)** Make sure to add a space between the ID and the tag name.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=73)** Then the equal sign.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=76)** Add a pair of quotes and then give it a name.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=78)** I'll call mine highlight because I'm going to be adding a yellow background color to the element.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=84)** In the CSS panel, don't worry about the styles that are already there.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=88)** For now, let's add the ID selector, using the number sign followed by the ID name.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=95)** Then add the curly brackets, and we'll use the background property to add a color.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=101)** I will go with yellow.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=104)** Now the style is applied to just the first paragraph.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=108)** The same ID value should only be used once per page, but if I were to add this highlight ID to another element, the browser will apply the style even though it's actually an error.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=122)** According to the specs, the same ID value should only be used once per page.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=128)** Even though the browser applied the style this time, it's important to write valid code to avoid unexpected results.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=136)** Some developers prefer not to use IDs at all for CSS 'cause it's not flexible, and there are other options.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=142)** But as long as you follow the syntax rules, it is valid.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=146)** So whether you use IDs or not, it's a personal choice.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/id-selectors?u=76281980&t=149)** We'll be using this pen again in the next lesson, so keep it open.
