#### [Class selectors](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] When type selectors are too general but you'd need more flexibility than IDs.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=4)** There's another option.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=5)** The class selector, which matches to one or more elements based on the class attribute value.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=11)** Similar to IDs, to use a class, it must first be added to the [[HTML]] tag and just like IDs, the value is defined by you.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=20)** But when used as a CSS selector, it starts with a period.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=24)** The same class name can be reused throughout the page by adding the same value to any number of elements.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=30)** The selector only matches to the class name, so it doesn't even have to be the same type of element.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=36)** You can also use multiple classes with the same element.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=39)** The order doesn't matter but each class name must be separated by a space and contained within the quotation marks.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=47)** For example, any element with either the fancy or highlight class name will have the corresponding styles applied to it.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=54)** If the element contains both class names then both styles will be applied.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=59)** To specify a style only when both classes are present, the class selectors are combined with no space.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=67)** This technique can be useful when you want to create styles that can be used independently while also creating specific styles using existing class names.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=77)** Different selector types can also be combined.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=80)** Let's go back to the code pen example from the last lesson.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=84)** In this pen, I've added three class styles in the CSS panel.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=89)** Let's apply these one by one to the HTML to see how classes are applied.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=94)** First, add the large text class to the first paragraph.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=99)** So, we'll add a space class="large-text.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=111)** Now, we can see the larger font size has been applied only to the first paragraph.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=117)** Now let's add the button class to the first button.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=125)** Again, this style is now only applied to the element that the class attribute has been added to but let's say I want the large button to have the button style and the large textile.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=138)** I can do that by adding both classes to the element.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=143)** I'll start by adding the button class, then add a space and then I'll add the large text style.
>

>

>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=181)** Now we can see that the button text is bolded and this is only applied if both classes exist within the same element.

> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/class-selectors?u=76281980&t=189)** Using classes is common in CSS because it allows for flexibility and can be used once or multiple times.

#### [Descendant combinators and selector lists](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When writing [[HTML]], tags are nested inside of other tags, which creates a hierarchy known as the [[Document Object Model (DOM)|Document Object Model]] or DOM for short.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=9)** Elements nested at the first level create a parent-child relationship.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=13)** Deeper levels create an ancestor-descendant relationship.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=17)** Elements nested within the same parent are sibling elements.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=20)** Descendant combinators are used to select nested elements by combining two selectors, one targeting an ancestor element, and the other a descendant element.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=30)** It must be separated by a space.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=33)** For example, section, space, p will select only paragraphs inside of a section element.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=41)** Let's open up a [[CodePen]] example to see the different ways we can use a descendant combinator.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=47)** Let's start by uncommenting the color value in the a selector.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=53)** Delete the slash and asterisk to add the style back in or use the keyboard shortcut, Command or Control + forward slash.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=61)** This is a type selector, so it will apply to all the links anywhere in the document.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=67)** The next selector, section, space, a is a descendant combinator.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=73)** This style will be applied to any links inside of a section element.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=78)** We can go more than two levels to create a more specific pattern to match.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=83)** Section p a will select only links contained within a paragraph contained within a section element.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=93)** We can also use different types of selectors.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=96)** Container a is also a descending combinator but it uses a class and type selector.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=103)** If we change the section tag to article, this style would still apply because it's matching to the class named container.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=111)** The more selectors you use, the more specific the pattern.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=114)** To avoid overly specific selectors, it's recommended to limit descendant combinators to two or three levels.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=121)** Now let's talk about another type of combinator, a selector list.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=126)** Selectors can be grouped to apply shared styles to more than one selector at once by using selector lists.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=133)** The syntax looks similar to descendant combinators but each selector must be separated by a comma.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=140)** Each selector in the grouped list is independent of the other.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=144)** So when using descendant selectors in a selector list, make sure to declare the full combination.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=150)** Grouping selectors can make your CSS more efficient because it reduces the number of declarations.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=156)** It's also easier to update one declaration block.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=159)** This is a small example, so you're only saving a couple of lines but as your CSS file gets longer and longer, every little bit helps.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/descendant-combinators-and-selector-lists?u=76281980&t=168)** Learning when to use each type of selector will take time but we'll continue to practice using them as we build out our projects.

#### [The background property](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we have a variety of selector options to choose from, let's prepare for an upcoming project update and talk about the background property.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=8)** We've already used this style in a few examples to change the background color of an element, but background is actually shorthand for many more background styles.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=18)** Let's open a code pen example to see how these properties work.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=22)** The longhand syntax for setting the background color of the element is background-color.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=27)** Let's add that style in for images.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=31)** If it's for presentation only, we can use the background property.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=35)** If it's part of the content, like the project thumbnails, then it should be added to the [[HTML]] with the IMG tag.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=41)** Otherwise, we can use the background-image property.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=45)** The value is the URL function value.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=49)** This is where we add the path to the image within the parentheses using the same rules for linking images we discussed in chapter one.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=58)** If the image file is smaller than the area it's applied to, the image will repeat horizontally and vertically to fit the entire area.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=66)** To prevent the image from repeating, use the background-repeat property and set it to no-repeat.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=72)** The background color will be visible where the image does not cover.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=76)** By default, the background image will align to the top left of its container.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=82)** Background-position is used to define the position relative to its container.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=88)** A single value will position the image from the left of the container.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=92)** Right now, I have it set to 10 pixels, but any length value can be used.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=97)** The top value will default to 50%, so the item is placed in the middle of the container.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=104)** Adding a second value will allow you to specify the position from the top.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=108)** You can also use different length units together.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=112)** Let's try adding 10%.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=115)** There are also five keyword values.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=120)** Instead of numbers, we can use top or bottom.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=126)** We can add a second value, left or right, or we can use center.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=138)** To make an image cover the entire area, you can use background-size.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=143)** First value sets the width, so a value of 100% will stretch the image to fill the container horizontally.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=150)** A second value will change the height of the image.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=153)** Setting it to 100% will make the image span the full height to fill the space.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=158)** but if the space does not have the same aspect ratio as the image, it will appear distorted.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=164)** Another option is to use the keyword cover.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=170)** The value will fill the container space, but to maintain the aspect ratio, the part of the image that doesn't fit into the container will be clipped.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=178)** Also, since cover is meant to fill the space, the background image will not repeat by default, but if you change it to something else like 50%, then it will.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=189)** Let's put it back to cover.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=194)** Background-size is not a perfect solution, especially if an important part of the image is not shown.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=199)** However, it does fill the space, and you can adjust the background-position property to move a specific part of the image within the viewable part of the container.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=209)** For example, setting it to right will ensure that the right side of the image is always visible.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=216)** Also, this image appears blurry because I'm using an image file that is smaller than the area, so it's being stretched out to be much larger.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=224)** In an actual project, we would use the image optimization techniques discussed in chapter one.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=230)** Now that we've looked at using the longhand syntax, let's go back to the shorthand syntax.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=235)** When using the shorthand property, order doesn't matter, except when using background-size.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=243)** It must be declared with a background position value and separated with a forward slash.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=249)** If no background-position value is being used, this shorthand syntax will not work.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=255)** To use the shorthand syntax with background-size but without background-position, add the longhand background-size declaration after the shorthand declaration.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-background-property?u=76281980&t=266)** There are additional background properties and value types that are not necessary for our course project or not as commonly used, but if you'd like to learn more, check out the full documentation on MDN.

#### [Project: Adding background styles](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this project exercise, we'll be adding the background image to the homepage and some background styles to the footer as well.

> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=10)** Although the Resume page also uses a background image, this style will require learning about a few more concepts to add the image only to the top, so we'll just leave this for now.

> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=101)** That way if something doesn't work as intended, I won't have to hunt through a long list of styles to figure it out.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=107)** Right now, I can only see a portion of my image, and that's because the size of your image file, screen size, and resolution will determine how much of the image you'll see.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=118)** If your screen size is larger than the height or width of the image file, it will repeat to fill the page.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=124)** So let's go back to the editor.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=127)** Even though my image is more than large enough to fill the page, let's add the no-repeat property just to be safe.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=133)** Don't forget to add the space between multiple values.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=136)** Now we'll add background size to fit the image to the dimensions of the viewport, but reminder, we can't add background-size to the shorthand syntax without using background position.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=146)** So let's just add it underneath and we'll give it a value of cover.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=153)** Save the file and check the update.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=157)** Now the width of the image will fit to the width of the viewport.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=161)** Also, it's flexible to any changes to the size of the viewport.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=166)** This is the flexibility that we want, but right now, to maintain the aspect ratio of the image, the height automatically resizes and doesn't cover the bottom area anymore, even though we use cover for the background size.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=180)** But don't worry about this just yet, we'll update this style later when we learn a few more concepts.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=186)** Adding the background image has added some visual interest, but now the text is hard to read.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=192)** To fix this, we can add a background color behind the text.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=195)** I'd also like to add a little opacity to it so we can still see the image through it just a little bit.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=202)** To do this, we'll add a container element just around the content, then we'll apply the background color to the container.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=209)** Let's go back to the index.[[HTML]] file.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=213)** The container needs to be added around just the header and main content area.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=218)** The footer will be styled separately.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=221)** I'm going to collapse the main content area just to make it a little easier to wrap this new container div around it.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=230)** We'll be using the div element since we don't need to add any semantic content.

>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=252)** This will probably mess up your HTML indentations, so let's take a moment to reformat.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=259)** I'll highlight the whole block and just Tab it in once.
>

>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=282)** In the color lesson, we talked about adding opacity with the hex values versus the RGB function value.
>

>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=297)** So for the background color, I'll use the shorthand background property again.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=302)** And for color, I'll be using white.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=304)** So the RGB value is pretty easy to remember.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=308)** Zero is black and 255 is white.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-background-styles?u=76281980&t=311)** So I can set three values to 255 to make it white.

#### [Pseudo-classes and pseudo-elements](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So far, we've gone over different ways to select specific elements, but with pseudo-class and pseudo-element selectors we can select a specific state or a specific part of an element.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=12)** Pseudo-classes target elements in a specific state.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=15)** For example, the first child element or when the user interacts with it such as hovering over a link.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=22)** They're added to a selector with a colon and a keyword.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=26)** Pseudo-elements are used to style a specific part of an element, such as the first letter or the first line of a paragraph.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=35)** They're also combined with a selector but are added with double colons followed by the keyword.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=42)** Let's look at an example in [[CodePen]].
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=45)** A common style for online articles is to mimic the [[Typography]] often seen in magazines by styling the first paragraph or first sentence differently from the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]].
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=54)** Let's start by adding the first class to the first paragraph.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=59)** Inside the opening tag I'll add the class attribute equals quotes, and the class name first.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=67)** Now the first paragraph is displayed with the larger font size, but what if we end up adding a new paragraph before this one?
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=75)** It will no longer be the first paragraph.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=78)** Now the style will no longer be applied, but we could just move the class to the new first paragraph or we can use the first child pseudo class.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=88)** Let's add a new selector, starting with article.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=92)** Then we'll add a space, then p, to select the paragraphs within the article tag.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=98)** Then we'll add the pseudo class, colon, no space, first dash child.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=104)** This will narrow the selection down to the first paragraph.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=108)** Add the curly brackets, then a style.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=111)** I'll use color and set it to red to make a different style from the first class.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=117)** Now you can add or remove paragraphs and the style will always be applied to whichever is the current first paragraph.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=125)** But what if I decided to add an H1 tag to include a title?
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=130)** The pseudo class will no longer work.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=132)** P first child means it has to be the first child of the article element and AP tag.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=139)** By adding the H1, the first paragraph is now the second child element within article, but there is another pseudo class we can use instead.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=149)** First of type, let's update the selector, first dash of dash type.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=159)** with this selector, Now it will look for the first P element rather than the first child element.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=166)** Now, let's say you want to change just the first letter rather than the whole paragraph.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=171)** Let's use the first letter pseudo-element to style only the first letter of the paragraph.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=178)** For pseudo-elements, we need to start with two colons, then first dash letter.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=187)** Now the style is applied to the first letter in each paragraph, but if we wanted to style just the first letter in the first paragraph, we can do that by combining selectors.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=199)** Let's put first of type back in, but we'll put it before first letter.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=204)** So we'll add one colon, first dash of dash type.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=211)** Now this will apply the style to the first letter of the first paragraph within the article element.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=218)** Pseudo-classes and pseudo-elements are useful, but they have very specific purposes as we've just seen in the demo.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=225)** Also, you'll start to see that there are often more than one way to do the same thing.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=229)** In these examples, using a class would have also worked.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=233)** To target just the first letter, we could have also wrapped a span element around it and added a class to use the selector.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=244)** With classes, you just have to remember to move or add them depending on if or how the content changes.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=250)** Sometimes there are best practices for doing things and sometimes it's just a matter of personal preference.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=258)** There are quite a few more pseudo-classes and pseudo-elements.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/pseudo-classes-and-pseudo-elements?u=76281980&t=262)** To learn more about them, you can find them in the MDN web docs, which is located in the sidebar of the reference guide.

#### [Inheritance and the cascade](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In CSS, styles can be inherited from ancestor elements to the descendant elements, which means styles can be applied to multiple elements with just one line of code.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=11)** For instance, all page content is contained inside the body tag so it's common to define shared styles using body as the selector for all the descendant elements to inherit.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=21)** This is more efficient than applying the style directly to each element.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=26)** Inheritable styles are mainly related to [[Typography]], like font, family, or color.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=31)** Non-inheritable styles are usually related to positioning elements or sizing, like width or height.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=38)** But how do you know exactly which properties are inheritable or not inheritable?
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=43)** Well, you can check the CSS documentation.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=46)** To find information about a specific property, I usually just do a web search with the keywords.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=52)** For example, if I'm searching for the color property, I'll add that to my search plus the language, in this case, it's CSS, and as seen many times throughout this course, I tend to check the MDN Docs first, so I'll also include that in my search.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=71)** Here's the MDN Docs as my first search result.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=74)** Let's open the page.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=76)** Here are some explanations and examples of the syntax.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=80)** If you scroll down to formal definition, we'll see a field that says inherited, yes.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=89)** Now we know this property is inheritable.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=92)** You don't need to memorize all these details about each property.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=96)** Reading documentation and tutorials are a common part of doing [[Web Development]].
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=101)** Another important concept to learn about is the cascade in [[Cascading Style Sheets (CSS)|cascading style sheets]], which refers to how style rules are applied based on the source order.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=110)** The browser reads style sheets from top to bottom.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=113)** If there are conflicting rules applied to the same element, the declaration that was loaded last will take precedence whether they're in the same or separate declaration blocks.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=124)** As your CSS files get larger, it's easy to forget a certain style has already been used and accidentally added again, so this is actually a common error.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=132)** To reduce these types of issues try organizing your CSS into related blocks.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inheritance-and-the-cascade?u=76281980&t=137)** This is what we'll be doing in our course project.

#### [Specificity](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We talked about the cascade, which determines that the last style rule takes precedence when styles conflict.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=6)** However, when different types of selectors are used, source order is overridden by a concept called specificity.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=14)** Specificity refers to the rules which determine which CSS style takes precedence when styles conflict but it's based on the type and number of selectors used.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=24)** With some having more weight than others, the higher the specificity, the more priorities a selector has when applying styles to an element.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=33)** Here's how the selectors are weighted from highest to lowest specificity, ID, class, type, then universal.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=42)** These values are calculated based on the selector type and the number of each type of selector used.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=49)** Here's how the specificity calculations work.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=52)** It's broken down into a three category count.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=56)** The number of ID selectors, the number of class selectors, and the number of type selectors.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=62)** The numbers for each count are then concatenated which means that they're joined together, not added.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=69)** This will give us the final specificity value.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=72)** In this example, this selector contains no IDs, one class, and one type selector for a concatenated value of zero, one, one.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=84)** The higher the value, the higher the specificity.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=87)** So in this example, id p has the highest specificity because there's one ID and one type selector resulting in a value of one, zero, one.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=99)** Even though a selector like ul, li, a has three selectors, they're all type selectors.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=105)** So the final concatenated value is only zero, zero, three.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=110)** The universal selector isn't included in this count so their value is zero, zero, zero.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=115)** Don't worry if these calculations seem overwhelming.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=118)** It's good to have an understanding of how this works but you don't need to know the exact selector specificity value to write CSS effectively.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=126)** It just takes some time to practice.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=128)** Inheritance, cascade, and specificity all control health styles are applied to [[HTML]] and each concept applies these rules differently.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=137)** So let's do a recap.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=139)** When an inheritable property is added to an element, the descendant elements will inherit the style as well.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=145)** Because of the cascade rule, the source order also matters.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=149)** The declaration that is loaded last will take precedence.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=153)** Unless you use a more specific selector.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=156)** It will override both the inherited styles and the cascade rule.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=160)** There is one exception to all these rules.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=164)** The important flag.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=166)** This keyword overrides all these rules to become the most specific rule, regardless of the cascade, inheritance, or specificity.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=176)** The syntax starts with an exclamation mark in the keyword important.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=180)** It's added to the end of the style declaration just before the semicolon.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=185)** The only way to override an important style is to use a more specific selector with another important flag, so this can get pretty messy.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=193)** In rare cases, it may be necessary to use important such as when working with a third party framework.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=200)** That doesn't allow access to the CSS file.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=202)** However, this should only be used as a last resort.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=208)** Understanding how to use important can be helpful if you encounter it.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=212)** But it's generally considered bad practice since it doesn't follow any rules.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/specificity?u=76281980&t=216)** In most cases, following the rules of the cascade, specificity, and organization will prevent conflicts between styles.

### 3. The CSS Box Model

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Intro to the CSS box model](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-the-css-box-model?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-the-css-box-model?u=76281980&t=0)** - Now that we've gone over some core CSS concepts let's continue to build on that understanding by exploring the CSS box model.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-the-css-box-model?u=76281980&t=8)** An important concept that explains how [[HTML]] elements are displayed in the browser.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-the-css-box-model?u=76281980&t=14)** When we look at a webpage, we see text and images but to the browser, every single element is displayed as a rectangular box, whether it's an image, a paragraph, or even a link inside of a paragraph.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-the-css-box-model?u=76281980&t=28)** Each of those HTML elements is an individual box.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-the-css-box-model?u=76281980&t=32)** The CSS box model is a set of rules that define how these boxes are sized and how much space they take up.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-the-css-box-model?u=76281980&t=39)** In this chapter, we'll talk about the five properties that make up the box model width, height, padding, margin, and border.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-the-css-box-model?u=76281980&t=48)** This is the foundation of layout on the web.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-the-css-box-model?u=76281980&t=51)** So before we can incorporate methods like Floats, Flexbox, or Grid, we first need to understand the box model.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[HTML]] (2)
> **Env Vars:** css (3), html (2)
> **Definitions:** is an  (1), is a  (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Speakers:** - now (1)

#### [The box model properties](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Every element is displayed as a box in the browser.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=3)** The CSS box model describes how these elements are displayed, which consists of four parts.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=9)** The content box contains the content added using the [[HTML]] tags.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=14)** The padding box surrounds the content and adds space inside of the element.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=20)** The border box surrounds the padding area and the margin box surrounds the entire element.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=27)** These boxes are represented by five CSS properties: width, height, padding, margin, and border.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=34)** Let's review the syntax and usage.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=37)** The width and height properties are used to set the width and height of an element's content box.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=42)** They can be used with keywords, length and percentage values.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=46)** You can also set a minimum or maximum value with min-width, max-width, min-height, and max-height properties.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=54)** The padding property defines the space inside of an element and around the content box.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=59)** It can be defined with length or percentage values.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=62)** All values must be positive numbers.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=65)** If an element already has default padding, setting it to zero will remove the padding space.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=71)** Also, a zero value can be used with or without a unit.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=74)** Padding can be defined using one to four values.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=78)** One value applies equal padding space to all sides.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=82)** Two values applies the first value to the top and bottom and the second value to the left and right.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=90)** Multiple values must be separated by a space.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=93)** Three values applies the first value to the top, the second value to the right and left, and the third value to the bottom.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=102)** Four values follows a clockwise order with the first value for the top, the second for the right, the third for the bottom, and the fourth for the left.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=112)** The longhand properties, padding-top, padding-right, padding-bottom and padding-left can be used to set the padding for a specific side and are only defined with one value.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=123)** The margin property defines the space around an element.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=126)** It's also used with the length and percentage values but they can be positive or negative numbers.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=132)** You can also use the keyword auto.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=134)** I'll talk a little more about this in an upcoming lesson.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=137)** The shorthand and longhand syntax rules are the same as padding.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=141)** It can also be used with one to four values.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=145)** The longhand properties adds the margin to a specific side.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=148)** Though the browser may view each element as a box, there are no actual visual borders, but the border property can be used to display a border style, which is added between the margin and padding of an element.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=161)** The border property is also shorthand for border-width, border-style, and border-color.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=167)** When using the shorthand syntax, the order of the values do not matter.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=172)** Each value though must be separated by a space.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=175)** For border-width, you can use any length values or the keywords thin, medium, or thick.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=181)** For border-color, any type of color value can be used.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=185)** Border-style is only used with keyword values.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=189)** None and hidden will display no border and the other keywords will display different styles.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=194)** For example, double will show two solid lines while dotted will show a dotted border.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=201)** Each of these longhand properties are actually shorthand for more longhand properties.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=206)** For example, border-width is shorthand for border-top-width, border-left-width, border-right-width and border-bottom-width.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=215)** These longhand properties can get pretty specific.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-box-model-properties?u=76281980&t=218)** So I would suggest referring to the MDN documentation if you would like to dive a little deeper.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **Env Vars:** css (2), html (1), mdn (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (2)
> **Documentation:** mdn (1)

#### [Box-sizing and the box model fix](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] Using different box model properties affects the overall size of the element.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=5)** This behavior can be managed with the box-sizing property, which defines how the total width and height of an element are calculated.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=12)** There are two values, content-box and border-box.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=16)** The default box-sizing value is content-box.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=19)** The width and height values apply to the content-box of the element.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=24)** Any added border or padding styles will increase the element's total size.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=30)** Margin adds space around the element, so it's not included in the total size of the element, but it does affect how much space the element takes up.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=38)** When the border-box value is used, the width and height will still be applied to the content-box.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=44)** However, when padding and border styles are added, the content-box shrinks to maintain the same overall size as defined by the width and height properties.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=55)** Again, margin is not included in these adjustments, since it adds space around the element.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=61)** Let's look at a code pen demo to see how this works.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=65)** Here are two boxes.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=67)** The first one has the default box sizing value of content-box, and the second one has a value of border-box.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=73)** Usually, you don't need to declare a default style, but I just added it here to make it easier to see for this demo.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=80)** Right now, the width, height, and margin have been set.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=83)** Margin will have no effect on the box sizing, so the size of the elements for both boxes is still 200 by 100 pixels.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=92)** Also, note that the text is currently aligned to the top left edge of its container.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=98)** Now let's add the padding style in.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=100)** We can see that it's been added to both boxes, because the text is no longer lined up to the edge of the box.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=106)** The padding has been added around the content-box, but now, box 1 is larger than box 2.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=114)** There's now 200 pixels of width, plus 20 pixels of padding on the left and right sides, which equals 240 pixels for the total width.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=124)** And for the height, it's set to 100 pixels, plus 20 pixels of padding on the top and bottom for a total of 140 pixels for the height of the element.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=135)** For box 2, using a value of border-box will decrease the size of the content-box, shrinking it just enough to make space for the padding.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=144)** So the total size of box 2 is still 200 by 100 pixels.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=150)** The same effect will happen when adding a border style.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=154)** If I wanted box 1 to remain the same overall size as what was initially set, we'll need to reduce the width and height values to account for the padding and border.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=165)** So for the width, it would be 200 minus 20 pixels of left padding, 20 pixels of right padding, minus 10 pixels of border on the left and 10 pixels of border on the right, which equals 140 pixels.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=180)** I'll add it to the box 1 selector, so we can compare its size to box 2.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=185)** For the height value, change it to 40 pixels to make room for the top and bottom padding and border styles.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=192)** So while you can make these adjustments, it's generally easier to size elements using the border box behavior, because you won't need to adjust the width and height every time you add padding or border styles.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=204)** Maintaining the same width is important when fitting multiple elements into a specific space.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=210)** For example, with the default box sizing of content-box, trying to add four 100 pixel elements won't fit in a 400-pixel container when padding or border is added.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=223)** So when starting a project, it's common to add what's often referred to as the box model fix, which is a CSS code snippet included in your CSS file.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=234)** This will apply the border box value to all the elements on the page.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=238)** The box sizing is first applied to the root element and is one of the special scenarios where the universal selector is used to make all the descendant elements and pseudo elements inherit this style.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=250)** This fix was first documented by Paul Irish.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/box-sizing-and-the-box-model-fix?u=76281980&t=253)** If you'd like to learn more about the history and changes to the fix over the years, check out the extra resources included in the links.PDF file.

#### [Inline, block, and display](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=0)** - As we've been building out our projects and doing [[CodePen]] examples, you may have noticed that [[HTML]] elements are arranged in certain ways.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=8)** For example, why do links line up side by side while headings and paragraphs stack on top of each other?
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=14)** That's because every HTML element falls into two categories, block or inline, which determine how they occupy space on the page.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=24)** Block elements are the same height as the content contained between their tags, but they span the entire width of their containing element even if the content itself does not.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=35)** This is why block elements always start on a new line.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=39)** Most elements are block elements.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=41)** Some commonly used ones are paragraphs, headings and sectioning elements like article and section.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=48)** Inline elements are the same size as their content.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=51)** They're also displayed in a line, side by side starting from the left.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=57)** The elements will wrap to the next line only if the content can't fit on the same line.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=62)** Some common inline elements are anchor tags, which are used for links span and strong.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=69)** The CSSS display property can be used to change the default behavior of block and inline elements using one of three values, block, inline or inline block.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=79)** Let's pull up an example in CodePen.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=83)** A quick way to check if an element is block or inline is to see how it's displayed when two elements are written consecutively in the HTML markup.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=91)** Does the element display on a new line or does it display on the same line?
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=96)** In this example, the divs are displayed on their own line, even though the content doesn't span all the way across, so they must be block elements.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=105)** The span elements are inline since they are lined up side by side to each other.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=110)** Let's add the background style for both the div and span just to make it a little easier to see what type of element they are.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=118)** If the color stretches all the way across the container it's a block element.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=122)** If it only spans the width of its content then it's an inline element.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=126)** Now let's take a look at how the box model properties are displayed with block and inline elements.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=133)** For block elements, setting the width and height will change the size of the element.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=139)** For inline elements, adding width and height will have no effect.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=144)** For block elements, setting margin adds space around the element.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=149)** That's why the background color doesn't extend to this space.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=153)** It will also push the element from its original position to accommodate the added space.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=159)** For inline elements, even when margin is added to the top and bottom the space is only displayed on the left and right sides.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=169)** Inline elements will shift to the left and right to accommodate the added space, but they will maintain their vertical positioning.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=176)** For block elements, padding adds space inside the element and also adjusts its positioning to fit the added space.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=185)** When adding padding to the inline element the space will be displayed on all four sides, but just like margin, it will still maintain its vertical position and if it takes up more space than what's available it will overlap the surrounding elements.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=200)** We'll see the same display behavior for block and inline element when the border style is added.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=208)** These default behaviors can be changed with the display property.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=212)** Let's set the display value to block for the inline elements.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=217)** This will apply all box model properties as they would be applied for block elements.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=223)** The same is true when setting display inline to a block element.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=230)** It will now display with the characteristics of inline elements.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=235)** There is one more display value that we can use to apply a mix of characteristics of both inline and block elements.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=242)** The value is aptly called inline block.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=246)** Let's change the display value for the div element to inline block.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=251)** With this style all the box model properties are applied, just like block elements, but the elements are displayed side by side, just like inline elements.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=260)** This is a good example of separating content from style.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/inline-block-and-display?u=76281980&t=264)** You may want your elements to display on a new line or on the same line, but instead of picking an element that looks a particular way choose the most semantic HTML element for the content and change the display with CSS.
