#### [Managing box model quirks](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So far, we've looked at a couple ways to test and inspect the different box model styles.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=6)** Now let's look at some known quirks, and how to manage them.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=10)** We'll start by focusing on the two paragraph elements, and discuss the behavior I mentioned in the previous lesson, margin collapsing.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=17)** I've added a background color to this example so we can see the space between the paragraphs, which is coming from a default margin style.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=26)** Since [[HTML]] was created for research documents, it makes sense for paragraphs to have this default style.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=32)** Otherwise, it would just be a big block of text.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=35)** And just note that not all HTML elements have default margin or padding styles.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=41)** But let's say you wanted to remove the space or make it smaller.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=45)** Currently this space is being occupied by the margin bottom of the first paragraph in the margin top of the second paragraph.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=54)** If we were to remove the bottom margin of the first paragraph using the first block style, it would look like there's been no change, and that's because this space is still coming from the top margin of the second paragraph.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=69)** But if we remove the top margin from the second paragraph, then we'll see the change.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=76)** There will no longer be any space between them.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=79)** Now let's shift the focus to the two inline elements, which by default are lined up side-by-side.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=85)** But notice that there's a little bit of space in-between them, and it's not a default margin style.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=91)** So setting it to zero would make no difference.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=94)** It's actually coming from the line break in the HTML.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=99)** If we were to put both of these tags on the same line, the space will be gone.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=106)** That technically fixes the issue, but writing all your HTML on one line will make it really hard to read.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=112)** So there are a couple other ways to get around this.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=116)** One way is to use margin left with a negative value to nudge it over.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=121)** I'll make a class for the second span because this is the one I want to move over.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=126)** I'll name it close gap, and then I'll add the selector to the CSS panel.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=132)** Then add a margin value of negative four pixels.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=136)** (keyboard typing) This will only work with margin because padding does not accept negative values.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=144)** Another option is to reduce the space between the elements using the font size property set to zero.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=150)** Font size is used to change the size of text, but it also applies to any characters including spaces.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=157)** Let's comment out this close gap style.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=161)** Then we'll create a container just for the span elements.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=164)** I'll use a div with a class of container.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=167)** (keyboard typing) Okay, so now I have both of my span tags enclosed in container, which is where I'll add a font size property with a value of zero.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=183)** I'll add the class to the CSS panel, and set it to font size zero.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=194)** This is an inheritable style, so it sets everything within the container element to zero, including the space between the span elements, and the content in the span elements.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=204)** So we'll have to re-declare the font size for the child elements.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=208)** So in the span declaration block, add the font size property there, and reset the value to 16 pixels.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=215)** Now the space between the span elements is gone, and the font size for the span elements are applied.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=222)** When there's no background color, the gap between inline elements isn't noticeable, but when using inline elements with a specific width inside a container that also has a specific width, the extra space may need to be removed.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/managing-box-model-quirks?u=76281980&t=235)** Newer layout techniques like flex box, and grid generally work better for this scenario, and will be covered later in the course.

#### [Using negative margin values](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Unlike other box model properties, margin can be used with both positive and negative values.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=6)** We just used a negative value in the last exercise but let's take a closer look at how it all works.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=13)** In this example, we'll apply positive and negative values to the second box to see how the surrounding elements are affected by these values.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=22)** Let's start with a stacked example.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=24)** Adding a positive value for margin top and margin bottom is as we discussed in previous lessons.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=34)** It will push the surrounding elements away from the top and bottom sides.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=39)** But if we change the top value to negative 20, it will bring the proceeding element closer which will make it overlap.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=50)** The same applies when using a negative bottom value, except that the element below is brought closer.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=57)** Adding a positive margin right value doesn't show any change.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=61)** A negative value doesn't either.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=64)** That's because there are no other elements to the right that would be pushed out or pulled in.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=70)** If we add a margin right value to the side by side example below, we'll see the element to the right of it get pushed away.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=78)** Using a negative margin right value for the side by side example will cause the element to the right to be pulled in.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=86)** Back in the stacked example, the position margin left value will show a change.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=93)** The element itself will be pushed away from the left side.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=98)** A negative value will pull it back from the left.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=104)** For the side by side example, a positive top value pushes the element down.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=111)** A negative value will pull it up and for the margin bottom, a positive or negative value won't show any change because there are no elements below it to see anything get pushed or pulled.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=126)** And lastly, a positive margin value will push it away from the element on its left side.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=132)** A negative value will pull it over.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=135)** Negative margin values are best used to nudge elements just outside of their default position as we did with the previous inline element exercise.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=144)** If you need to move an element to a completely different location on the page, it's usually better to restructure the [[HTML]] or use a different layout technique.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=153)** This helps to maintain the natural flow of the page as much as possible.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/using-negative-margin-values?u=76281980&t=157)** We'll be discussing different layout techniques in the upcoming chapters.

#### [Center aligning with margin](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There's one more thing to know about margins.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=2)** This property can also be used with the auto value to center-align block elements.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=8)** Let's try this out in a [[CodePen]].
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=11)** To center-align the elements, the first thing we'll do is add a width to the section element.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=16)** Any value can be used, it just needs to be smaller than the viewport.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=20)** If the element spans all the way across, then there would be no need to center-align it.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=25)** I'll go with 400 pixels.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=30)** For the margin, the top and bottom value can be set to whatever you want.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=35)** If you don't want any additional space, just set it to 0.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=38)** Then set the left and right values to auto.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=42)** I'll be using the two value format, so 0 for the top and bottom and auto for left and right.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=49)** Make sure there's a space between the two values.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=52)** The horizontal space is now split evenly between the left and right margins, and the element is center-aligned.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=59)** Now, you might be thinking, if I set all four sides to auto, it should align the element horizontally and vertically, right?
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=67)** Unfortunately, margin just doesn't work that way, but this can be achieved with flexbox, which we'll be discussing later.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=75)** I'll just change the top and bottom value back to 0.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=78)** But wait a minute, why is there still a space at the top of the output panel?
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=83)** This can't be coming from the margin since I set it to 0, but it's actually a default margin on the body element, which surrounds all the content.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=94)** CodePen is a tool that was created to focus on isolated concepts so you don't have to include the body element in the [[HTML]] panel.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=102)** It's already included in the backend of the tool.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=105)** But we can still use it as a selector and remove the extra space around the output panel by adding a 0 margin to the body element.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=117)** Now, the default space has been removed.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=120)** When content spans across a webpage, it makes it hard to read on larger screens as we've seen in the current state of our course project.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=127)** So this technique is often used to keep the page content from spanning all the way across, while also ensuring that the content stays in the middle.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=135)** However, this method may pose a problem depending on how you want to style these blocks.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=141)** In this example, the background color only spans the width of the content.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=146)** But what if you want the background, color, or image to expand the entire width of the page, but keep the content in the middle?
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=154)** To accomplish this, create a container just for the content.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=158)** In the HTML panel, add a div around the content only.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=163)** We're going to use a div here because this element has no semantic meaning.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=167)** It's just being used as a container for styling purposes.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=173)** Take your closing tag and add it just after the last paragraph.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=179)** And I'm just going to fix the indentation.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=184)** Now, let's add a class to the div.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=186)** I'll call mine content-wrapper.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=190)** Then we'll add this to our CSS as our selector, .content-wrapper plus the curly brackets.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=200)** Now, we're going to move all of our styles here except for the background styles.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=208)** Now, the background color is applied to the whole section.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=212)** The content wrapper container is now used to center-align just the content.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/center-aligning-with-margin?u=76281980&t=217)** We'll be using this technique to align the content blocks in our projects.

> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-a-header-image?u=76281980&t=288)** But for this particular shorthand syntax, I generally like to keep the background size separate so I don't have to remember to change it if I decide not to use background position.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-a-header-image?u=76281980&t=299)** But it's totally up to you how you want to write it as long as it's valid.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-a-header-image?u=76281980&t=304)** Also, I'm sure you don't want to keep the placeholder text in the image area anymore, so let's remove that.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-a-header-image?u=76281980&t=310)** We still need to use the content property for the before pseudo element to work, but if you don't actually have any content you want to add, just leave the value blank, but keep the quotes.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-a-header-image?u=76281980&t=321)** Let's save the file and check this update for the last exercise of Chapter 3.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-a-header-image?u=76281980&t=329)** At this point, I'm pretty happy with the placement of my header image, but depending on what image you chose, you may want to adjust the height of the header or the positioning of the image.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-a-header-image?u=76281980&t=338)** We've added a lot of styles, but I'm sure at this point you're ready to start creating some page layout styles so that everything isn't just stacked on top of each other.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-a-header-image?u=76281980&t=348)** In the next two chapters, we'll cover a variety of layout techniques using float, position, flex box, and grid.

### 4. Layouts: Float and Position

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Intro to CSS Layout](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-layout?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-layout?u=76281980&t=0)** - CSS page layout techniques are used to change the position of elements on a webpage in relation to factors such as the default position within the normal page flow, other elements around them, and the browser viewport window.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-layout?u=76281980&t=14)** The normal flow is the default page layout, which is how it looks when you don't make any changes.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-layout?u=76281980&t=19)** This works for simple layouts, such as a blog, where content basically just flows from top to bottom.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-layout?u=76281980&t=25)** But for more complex layouts that require a sidebar or multiple columns and rows, we'll need to use CSS layout techniques to change the flow.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-layout?u=76281980&t=33)** CSS grid, flexbox, floats and position are methods used to change how elements are laid out in the CSS.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-layout?u=76281980&t=41)** Each tool has its own specific uses and benefits, and it's not uncommon to use a combination of layout methods in the same project.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-layout?u=76281980&t=49)** Knowing what each tool is designed for will help us choose the best one for the task at hand.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-layout?u=76281980&t=54)** In this chapter, we'll focus on layouts with the float and position properties.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** css (4)
> **Analogies:** such as (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Speakers:** - css (1)

#### [Layouts with the float property](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=0)** - Historically, the float property was used to create page layouts.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=4)** However, we now have grid and Flexbox, which provide better solutions.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=9)** Though the use of floats in layouts have decreased, you may still need to support them.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=13)** For example, you may end up working on a project that uses an old code base, often referred to as legacy code.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=19)** Plus, floats are still relevant today and can be used for simple layouts, so it's important to understand how they work.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=27)** Early websites mostly recreated print designs using the float property to create magazine style layouts with floated images and text flowing around those images.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=38)** Developers soon realized they could float other elements too, leading to float-based page layouts.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=45)** Most [[HTML]] documents are block level and are displayed at a full width, stacking on top of each other in the normal document flow.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=53)** The float property can be used to change the position of elements by floating them to the left or right side of their container.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=60)** However, this removes the floated elements from the normal flow, resulting in a change to the positioning of these surrounding elements.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=69)** Let's take a look at how this works in a [[CodePen]] example.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=73)** I've already added some border and background styles just to make it easier to see the boundaries of each element.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=79)** Let's start by adding float left to the image.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=84)** This will place the image to the left of its container.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=88)** Floated elements are taken out of the normal flow, so the next element, in this case, the paragraph, will move up to fill the area.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=96)** The space here between the paragraphs is the default margin style for p tags.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=101)** Speaking of margin, let's add some to the image to create some space around it.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=106)** The text is now flowing around the image, but the background color on the paragraph shows that it's actually filling up the whole space behind the image.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=115)** But the content contained within will make room for the floated element while filling up the remaining space around it.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=123)** Let's say we have a short paragraph where the height of the content is less than that of the floated image.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=128)** Let's comment out the long paragraph to see what happens.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=134)** Now, both of the short paragraphs will flow around the image since they both fit in the available space.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=140)** But what if we wanted only the first paragraph to wrap around the image?
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=144)** We can achieve that by clearing the float using the clear property.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=149)** First, determine where you want the layout to return to the normal flow.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=153)** If you only want the first paragraph to wrap around the image, then the clear property should be applied to the second paragraph.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=160)** This will return the layout to the normal flow from this point on.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=164)** I've added a clear class in the CSS panel with three possible values to try out to apply this style.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=169)** Add a class to the second paragraph tag with a value of clear.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=176)** Now let's look at the first option in the CSS panel.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=180)** If we add clear left, this will be used to clear left floats.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=185)** So this one works because the image was floated to the left.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=188)** The second paragraph is no longer flowing around the image and has returned to the normal flow.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=194)** The second option, right, will clear right floats.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=201)** So in this example, it won't work since the image is floated left.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=206)** The third option is the one I like to use, both, because it clears both left and right floats.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=211)** So if you change your float value, you won't need to change your clear value.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=216)** An important thing to note is that this method only works when the clear property is applied to a sibling element that follows the floated element.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/layouts-with-the-float-property?u=76281980&t=224)** But there are other techniques for different scenarios that we'll continue to explore.

#### [Clearing floats](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] In the previous float exercise we applied the clear property to a specific element to return the page back to its normal flow.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=7)** But what if there is no element to apply the clear to?
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=11)** In this example, the blue box is floated to the left, taking it out of the normal flow.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=17)** As a result, the parent element shown with the black border will not recognize the height of the floated element, which will cause it to overflow outside of the parent element.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=27)** If all the child elements are floated, then the parent element's height will collapse to zero.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=33)** Elements that are not in the same container as the float will also be affected.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=37)** If there is enough space, the elements that come after the float will stack underneath in the available space.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=45)** Their child elements will also flow around the floated elements.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=49)** When there's no element in the same container as the float to which a clear style can be applied to, a self clear must be applied to the parent element instead.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=59)** There are three ways to do this.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=61)** Option one, use the overflow property with a value of auto or hidden.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=66)** Although this property has been used in the past for clearing floats, its main purpose is to control the display of content that's larger than its container.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=75)** When size dimensions are added to a container element, the content may sometimes extend beyond those dimensions, causing the content to flow outside of its container.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=84)** Using the hidden value with the overflow property will clip the overflow of content.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=89)** While this may be okay for decorative content such as images, it's not recommended for text because the clipped content is hidden.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=97)** The auto value adds a scroll bar but only when there's an overflow of content.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=102)** To clear floats, any overflow value can be used.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=105)** But this technique can result in clipped content or unintended scroll bars when working with overflowing content.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=112)** There's also a second option using the clearfix hack, which is a snippet of CSS that has been created and updated by the developer community.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=122)** The snippet looks like this.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=124)** Similar to the before pseudo element used in our projects for the header image, after places a generated element after the parent element.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=133)** Then the clear property is applied to this generated element, resetting the following elements to a normal flow.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=140)** Although this method is called the clearfix hack, the clearfix in this CSS snippet is just a class name.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=146)** If you want to use a different name, you can.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=148)** Like any other class name, it just needs to be consistent in both CSS and [[HTML]].
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=154)** These types of fixes were often created to support older browsers, mostly old versions of Internet Explorer.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=161)** But with [[Microsoft]]'s end of support for IE and a push to use their modern browser edge, supporting older browsers is now less of an issue, which leads us to the third and newest method, involving using the display property with a value of flow root.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=176)** This creates a new block formatting context or BFC for short.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=181)** This just basically means that all the elements will be contained within the parent element, including the floated ones, so you don't have to worry about any floated elements poking out of the container.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/clearing-floats?u=76281980&t=192)** With the clearfix hack being more than 15 years old and the adoption of modern browsers, flow root is the best solution since it was designed to be used this way rather than as a workaround.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[HTML]] (1), [[Microsoft]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** css (3), html (1), bfc (1)

> **Analogies:** such as (1), similar to (1)

> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-float-images-and-text?u=76281980&t=158)** This will keep all the child elements floated or not floated within their containers.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-float-images-and-text?u=76281980&t=164)** Let's save the file and check these updates.

#### [The position property](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] The position property is a technique used to position elements by changing the flow of the document.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=6)** It has five values, static, relative, absolute, fixed, and sticky.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=11)** The default position for elements is static, which means they are not positioned.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=16)** For the other values, the box offset properties, top, right, bottom, and left must also be used to specify the placement of the positioned elements.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=27)** Let's use a code pen example to see how these position values work.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=32)** Starting with relative positioning, scroll down to the relative class and uncomment the position relative style.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=41)** We won't see any changes yet because relative is the only positioned element that stays in the normal layout flow.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=48)** So none of the surrounding elements will be affected either but all positioned elements can be moved using the offset properties.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=57)** A top value of 20 pixels will offset the element 20 pixels from the top of its current position.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=64)** A bottom value will push it from the bottom of its current position, but if both top and bottom are declared, the bottom value will be ignored.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=73)** This is true for all the position values.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=76)** The right and left values will specify the right and left offset, but if both a right and left value are declared, the right value will be ignored.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=85)** This is also true when used with all the position values.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=89)** Now let's look at position absolute.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=92)** Add the style in and watch what happens to the fixed box.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=97)** It looks like it disappeared, but if I comment out the background color of the absolute box, we'll see the green fixed box behind it.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=105)** The fixed box was stacked below the absolute box but when the absolute box was removed from the normal flow, the fixed box moved up to fill the space.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=116)** Let's add the background color back in and add the right and bottom offset properties.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=123)** For the relative example, I used pixels but any length unit can be used.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=128)** So for the absolute example, I've used percentage values. By default, absolute positioning is relative to the body element.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=136)** Currently, the box is positioned 25% from the right and 5% from the bottom of the initial viewable area.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=144)** I say initial because if you scroll down, the box doesn't stay 5% from the bottom of the page.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=152)** It was 5% from the bottom of the page when the page was initially loaded.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=158)** When using position absolute, it's common to contain the element within a specific area.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=163)** This can be done by adding a position value to a parent or ancestor element.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=168)** So let's add position to the wrapper, which is displayed with the black border.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=175)** Any position value can be used but relative will contain the absolute box within this container without affecting the natural page flow.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=185)** Now it's 25% from the right and 5% from the bottom of the wrapper.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=191)** Now, if I add position relative to the parent element, which is the box with the red border, the absolute box will now be positioned to this container.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=201)** Elements with absolute positioning are relative to the nearest positioned container element.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=207)** If none exists, then it will be relative to the body.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=211)** Now let's scroll down to the fixed declaration block and add the fixed position style.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=216)** It is also removed from the normal flow which is why the gray sticky box has moved up and behind it.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=224)** Let's add in the offset properties then try scrolling the page.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=234)** Fixed positioning is always positioned relative to the view viewport and stays in the same spot even on page scroll. It's not affected by any positioned parent or ancestor elements.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=246)** Lastly, there is sticky positioning.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=249)** A new position value now supported by modern browsers.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=253)** Similar to relative positioning, using sticky value will not result in any changes until at least one offset property is added.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=266)** So let's add in the top value.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=268)** With sticky positioning, the element stays in its initial spot until you scroll the page.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=274)** Then when the offset value has been met, in this case, it's 10 pixels from the top, it becomes fixed.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=281)** When you scroll past, its containing element.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=283)** It will go back to its initial position.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=287)** It may be tempting to use the position property for creating page layouts since you can arrange the element in specific areas on the page.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=295)** But positioning shouldn't be used this way since it takes the element out of the normal flow with the exception of relative.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-position-property?u=76281980&t=303)** Stick to using position for styling smaller page components such as a fixed navigation bar rather than large page layout blocks.

#### [Position and z-index](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So far, we've talked about how elements can be placed along the X and Y axis, but there's actually a third axis, the Z axis.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=9)** When an [[HTML]] element is added within the same container, the elements are stacked in layers on the Z axis.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=17)** This is called the stacking context.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=20)** An element with a higher stack level is displayed in front of the element with a lower stack level.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=27)** The stacking context depends on the order, the type of the element, and how they're positioned.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=32)** The stacking order applies to elements that share a common containing element.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=38)** In this example, box one and box two are in their own stacking context within the section block.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=46)** The same goes for box three and box four, but the section blocks also have a common containing element, the body tag, which then [[Forms]] another stacking context.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=57)** Elements stack on top of each other by default, so most of the time, it's hard to see how they're stacked on the Z index unless they overlap each other.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=66)** So let's take a look at a [[CodePen]] example.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=70)** As I mentioned before, CodePen is meant for testing smaller code snippets.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=74)** To reduce redundancy, the HTML and body tags are already included within the tool on the backend, so we can't see the tags in the HTML panel, but we can still apply CSS to them.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=87)** The root element is shown with a gray background color and is followed by the body element, shown with a white background color.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=95)** These are always at the bottom of the stack because all other elements are nested inside of them.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=101)** For the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of the demo, I'll remove the display none style one by one so we can see the default stacking order.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=110)** After HTML and body, next in the stack are block elements in the order that they appear in the HTML.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=119)** So block one is stacked on top of body, then block two, since it comes after block one in the HTML.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=127)** By default, these elements will stack on top of each other on the Y axis.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=134)** So I've added margin styles to make them overlap so we can see how the elements stack on the Z axis.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=141)** The next layer in the stacking context are floated elements that are not positioned, then inline elements, and finally, on top of the stack are elements that are positioned.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=157)** The default stacking order can be changed using the Z index property, but the element must have a position applied to it for it to work.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=165)** Let's try moving block one from its current placement in the stack.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=168)** First, we'll need to add the position property.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=172)** I'll use a value of relative so the Z index property can be applied, but the element stays in the normal flow.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=180)** Just by adding position relative, the stacking level for block one has already changed, but the orange position box is still displayed on top of block one because it comes after it in the HTML.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=195)** Now, I'll add the Z index property to block one.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=198)** Any number value can be used to move it up to the top of the stack.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=204)** I'll give it a value of one.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=207)** If you use Z index to change the stacking order of multiple elements, a higher value means a higher stacking level.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=215)** This means we can control the order of the stack by using a larger or smaller number.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=220)** Let's make another one of these elements higher in the stack by using a higher Z index value.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=225)** I'll add position relative to the inline element.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=230)** Then I'll give it a Z index value of something that's higher.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=233)** Let's go with two.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=235)** Now this inline element is at the top of the stack.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/position-and-z-index?u=76281980&t=239)** Changing the stacking order with Z index is most useful for elements that always need to be displayed on top of other elements, such as dropdown menus, tooltips, or a pop-up box.

### 5. Layouts: Flexbox and Grid

#### [Intro to Grid and Flexbox](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-grid-and-flexbox?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-grid-and-flexbox?u=76281980&t=0)** - Flexbox and Grid are newer layout modules that allow for more advanced structures than previous layout techniques.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-grid-and-flexbox?u=76281980&t=7)** While they use similar syntax and some of the same properties, each has its own specialties.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-grid-and-flexbox?u=76281980&t=12)** Flexbox is often described as one dimensional 'cause it only aligns items on a single axis at a time, either rows or columns.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-grid-and-flexbox?u=76281980&t=22)** Grid, on the other hand, can be used to arrange items into both rows and columns at the same time, making grid layouts two dimensional.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-grid-and-flexbox?u=76281980&t=31)** But the differences don't mean that one is better than the other.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-grid-and-flexbox?u=76281980&t=33)** Grid and Flexbox have introduced new and flexible solutions for many different use cases.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-grid-and-flexbox?u=76281980&t=39)** Also, these are completely new layout modules.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-grid-and-flexbox?u=76281980&t=43)** This means new terminology, properties and rules.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-grid-and-flexbox?u=76281980&t=47)** I'll provide an overview of how to get started and cover the key concepts.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-grid-and-flexbox?u=76281980&t=50)** We'll be able to start implementing these techniques right away, but this is not something that can be mastered in just a day or two.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-grid-and-flexbox?u=76281980&t=57)** It will take some time and practice.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - flexbox (1)

#### [Intro to Flexbox](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Historically, float, and sometimes display and position, were used to create various page layouts.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=7)** While there are still uses for these properties the flexible box layout, or flexbox for short, has introduced more ways for aligning and sizing elements.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=16)** Let's dive into flexbox, beginning with the terminology.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=20)** The flex container refers to the parent element.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=23)** The flex items refer to only the child elements within the flex container.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=28)** Flex items are laid out along the main axis.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=32)** The direction is horizontal by default, so the flex items are arranged in rows, but the direction of the main axis can be changed to run vertically in columns using the flex direction property set to column.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=47)** The cross axis will always run perpendicular to the direction of the main axis.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=53)** Both axes also have start and endpoints, main start and main end for the main axis, cross start and cross end for the cross axis.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=62)** To use flexbox, the first step is to define the flex container.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=67)** Earlier we used the display property to change the behavior of inline and block elements, but display is also used to define the flex container with one of two values: flex or inline flex.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=79)** When display: flex is added to the parent element, each child becomes a flex item and will automatically be displayed in a row.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=88)** The flex items will also be the same size as their content, while the flex container still spans the width of its container.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=96)** If the display value is set to inline flex, the flex container will span the width of its content, the flex items, and displays inline to other flex containers.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=107)** Though the flex items resize themselves automatically to the size of their content, you can set specific sizes as well, which we'll talk about soon.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=115)** Also, with flexbox, the height of each flex item automatically adjusts to match the height of the tallest item, without requiring additional CSS.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=125)** If you're new to CSS, you are in luck.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=128)** Legacy techniques such as float required additional hacks to make equal height columns, but by using only the flex display value, we can already create basic column layouts.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-flexbox?u=76281980&t=139)** We'll continue to explore more flex properties for further customization and layout options.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** css (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Definitions:** refers to (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Flexbox: Orientation and ordering](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One useful feature of Flexbox is its ability to align items in either rows or columns.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=6)** The flex-direction property controls the direction of the main axis and also the order of the items.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=13)** It has four possible values.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=15)** Row, row-reverse, column, and column-reverse.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=20)** This property is added to the flex container.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=27)** Row is the default value.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=29)** The main start and main end depends on the writing mode of the document.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=33)** In left-to-right languages like English, main start and main end runs from left to right.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=39)** In right-to-left languages like Arabic, main start and main ends runs from right to left.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=45)** Using the column value with flex-direction changes the main axis to a vertical direction.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=51)** This displays the flex items in a column with each item stacked on top of each other.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=56)** The main start and main end properties run from top to bottom and expand to the width of their container.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=63)** The direction of the main start and main end can also be flipped.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=68)** Row-reverse displays a row of flex items with main start and main end running right to left.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=74)** Column-reverse displays a column of flex items with main start and main end running from bottom to top.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=82)** However, for accessibility reasons, using reverse values should not replace the ordering of the content.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=90)** This should only be used for visual styles since the ordering of the [[HTML]] itself is not changed.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=96)** Flex items are arranged on a single axis by default defined by the flex-wrap property.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=103)** The default value is nowrap, which means flex items will always stay on the same line.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=109)** If there are too few items to fill the container, the remaining space will be displayed.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=114)** If the total width of the items is larger than the container, they will shrink to fit.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=120)** But if the items can't shrink enough to fit in the container, the content will overflow.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=125)** This can be changed by setting flex-wrap to wrap.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=129)** This will cause the flex items to wrap to the next line if they don't fit instead of shrinking them to fit into one line.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=137)** But if there is extra space in the container in the last row, it will still be displayed.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=143)** There are other flex properties that we can use to control how items expand and shrink to fill the space.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=149)** We'll cover these in an upcoming lesson.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=152)** The direction of the wrapped items can also be changed using the wrap-reverse value.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=157)** Just note that only the cross start and cross end are reversed.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=161)** The items are still in the same order on the main axis.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=165)** Flex-wrap and flex-direction can also be defined together using the shorthand property flex-flow.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=172)** We'll continue to explore a few more flex properties, their syntax, and usage.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-orientation-and-ordering?u=76281980&t=176)** Then we'll apply these concepts in an example and a project update.

#### [Sizing with flex properties](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The main feature of the Flexbox layout is its ability to have the flex items expand or shrink to fit its container.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=7)** This is defined by the flex property, which is shorthand for flex-grow, flex-shrink and flex-basis.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=13)** Let's review the syntax and usage.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=16)** Flex-grow determines how items expand in the flex container.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=20)** It's defined with a unitless number value.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=24)** The default is zero, meaning items won't expand and any extra space won't be filled.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=30)** But a value of one will expand all the flex items equally to fill the container.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=35)** But the flex-grow value of one doesn't make all the flex items the same size.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=40)** If some flex items have more content than others they will still be displayed with different sizes.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=46)** However, the extra space is divided evenly among the flex items and added to the current size of each item.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=54)** The flex shrink property controls how flex items will shrink if they're larger than the container.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=60)** It also uses a unitless number value.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=64)** The default is one, which means that all the flex items will shrink by the same amount.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=69)** Setting the value to zero will prevent the flex items from shrinking at all.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=75)** If there isn't enough space in the container the flex items will overflow unless flex-wrap is set to wrap.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=81)** Flex basis sets the initial size of flex items with length, percentage or keyword values.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=88)** The default value is auto, which means flex items are displayed as the same size of its content.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=94)** It also sits the size of the flex items using the default box sizing value of content-box, which means any padding or border added to the flex item will make it larger, but most projects will have the box model fixed supplied, which we covered in the box model lessons.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=110)** These properties are usually defined using the shorthand flex property to ensure all values are set.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=116)** When using the shorthand syntax the order is flex-grow, flex-shrink and flex-basis.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=123)** Now, let's look at how this all works together.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=126)** First, define the display property in the container element to initiate a Flexbox layout.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=132)** Then apply the flex properties directly to the flex items.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=137)** You can do a lot with the flex property and there are a few more flex values we haven't explicitly discussed.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/sizing-with-flex-properties?u=76281980&t=143)** I would encourage you to check out the MDN documentation after getting comfortable with the basics.
