#### [Flexbox exercise](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've just covered some general usage and syntax for various Flexbox sizing properties.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=5)** Now let's take a closer look at how to use them in a [[CodePen]] example.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=10)** Let's start by adding display flex to define the flex container.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=14)** All the child elements have become flex items and align in a row.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=19)** They also become the same width as their content.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=23)** We can also see that there is extra space in the container.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=27)** Now let's look at how we can change the sizing by adding the flex property.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=32)** The first value, flex grow, defaults to zero.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=36)** That means it will not expand to fill any extra space.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=39)** If we change the value to one.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=41)** All the flex items will now expand by the same amount to fill up the space.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=47)** Since we're applying the same flex grow value to all of flex items, using a value of two or three or any number other than one won't make much of a difference.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=57)** But if I wanted one of the items to have twice the amount of extra space applied to it, I can do that by applying the style to just the specific flex item.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=67)** I'll add a second class of item one to the first item, item dash one.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=75)** Then we'll add in the flex grow property style with a value of two.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=80)** Now, the first item will get twice the amount of the remaining space applied to it as long as there's enough space to be distributed.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=87)** The second value in the flex property represents the flex shrink value.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=91)** The default is one, so if there isn't enough space in the container, all flex items will shrink by the same amount.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=99)** Let's add a width value set to something smaller.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=102)** I'll use 300 pixels.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=105)** Now we can see the items shrink to fill the space to stay in the same row.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=110)** If we were to change the value to zero, this means the items won't shrink at all, and since the container is smaller than the width of all the content, it will overflow.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=121)** We can set the flex wrap to wrap so the longer items will move to a second row.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=127)** In this case, even with the wrap, the longer block of text still overflows because it's set to not shrink.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=134)** This leads us to the third value, flex basis, which is used to set the initial size of the flex item.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=141)** It has a default value of auto, which means flex items are the size of its content.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=147)** If I change it to a hundred pixels, then all the flex items will be set to a minimum of a hundred pixels instead.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=154)** Now the overflowing content will fit in the container as long as it's at least 100 pixels wide.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=161)** We set the width of the container to 300 pixels and the flex basis to 100 pixels, so there should be enough room for three items per row.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=171)** So why is it only showing two?
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=173)** Well, that's because by default, the width of an element is sized using the content box.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=179)** If we were to set the box sizing property to border box, the width of the flex item will now include the padding and border when calculating its overall width.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=190)** Now, three items will fit in the first row, and since there are only two remaining items, the flex grow value of one is being applied to fill the remaining space.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=201)** The flex basis value applies to the width if the flex items are displayed as rows.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=207)** If I use flex direction and change it to columns, the flex basis will then apply to the height of the flex items.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=215)** If that felt like a lot to take in, that's because it is a lot.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=218)** Flexbox has given us many new layout options, and there's still more to learn, but you don't need to know everything to begin using it.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/flexbox-exercise?u=76281980&t=225)** In the upcoming lessons, we'll look at how to incorporate Flexbox into our project.

#### [Aligning flex items](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Prior to Flexbox, there were options for center-aligning elements horizontally, but getting elements to align vertically required a few hacks.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=9)** With Flexbox, two new properties were introduced to achieve this effect.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=14)** The justify-content property aligns the flex items on the main axis.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=18)** The align-items property aligns flex items on the cross axis.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=23)** To center-align a flex item vertically and horizontally, set the value to center for both properties, and that's it.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=30)** No workarounds required.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=32)** But these two properties can do a lot more than just center-align items.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=36)** They can also be used to distribute space between and around the items.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=41)** Let's look at a demo in [[CodePen]].
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=43)** Right now, we have three list items contained in an unordered list.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=48)** They're block items, so by default, they stack on top of each other and are the same width as the container.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=54)** Let's add display flex to initiate the flex layout and define the flex container.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=60)** Now, the items will be displayed in a row with each item the same width as the content and the same height as the container.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=68)** Let's add in the first style, justify content with a value of flex-start.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=74)** We won't see a change because this is the default value.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=78)** To justify a content property controls alignments of all items on the main axis, and flex-start aligns the flex items to the main start which is the left edge.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=89)** But if we change it to flex end, the items will now align to the main end, which is the right edge.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=96)** Center aligns the items in the middle.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=101)** Space between aligns the first and last item at the start and end of the axis then distributes the remaining space evenly among the items.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=112)** Space around distributes the same space on the left and right side of each item.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=117)** That's why it looks wider in the middle area because half of this space is added to the right of the first item and the other half is added to the left of the second item.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=129)** Space evenly distributes equals space between each items.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=134)** Unlike the flex-grow and flex-shrink properties, these alignment styles don't change the size of the flex items.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=141)** It just redistributes the space around them.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=145)** Align-items distributes space in the cross axis, which runs from top to bottom.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=151)** The default style displays the items stretched to the height of the container, which is represented by the stretch value.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=159)** So, by adding the value in now, we won't see a change.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=162)** Let's add in the center value instead.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=166)** Now the items are vertically aligned in the center, and since we removed the stretch value, the height of each flex item is now the same as the content.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=176)** Now, let's look at flex-start.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=178)** This value will align the items to the cross start of the container, which is the top, and flex end will align the items to the cross end, which is the bottom.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=188)** These are some of the commonly used values for these properties, but there are actually more values for both of these properties.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/aligning-flex-items?u=76281980&t=194)** To learn more, check out the extra resources included in the Links.pdf file.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[CodePen]] (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Intro to CSS Grid](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Before there was CSS Grid, grid-based layouts were a common part of [[Web Design]].
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=5)** Grids are divided into evenly spaced columns with gutters to add consistent spacing between them.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=11)** The grid then serves as a guide for laying out page components by arranging them within the columns.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=16)** CSS Grid is a layout method that makes it possible to create grid-based designs that previously required hacks and other workarounds.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=25)** Let's go over some grid terminology.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=28)** Similar to the Flexbox layout, the grid container is the parent element.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=32)** The direct child elements are the grid items.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=36)** To begin using a grid layout, start by defining the grid container with the display property with either the grid or inline-grid value.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=46)** Both will designate the child elements as grid items which will display in a single column.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=52)** When using the value of grid, the width of the items by default will span the width of its container.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=58)** When using the value of inline-grid the width will span the width of its content.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=63)** Similar to Flexbox, when using the value of grid the container will be displayed as a block element and stack on top of other block elements.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=73)** When using the value of inline-grid the container will be displayed inline next to other inline elements.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=80)** There are also many parts that make up the grid.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=83)** Grid lines are the horizontal and vertical lines that divide the grid into columns and rows.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=89)** They're also used to determine the position of grid items and are referred to by a numerical index or a custom name.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=96)** Both the vertical and horizontal grid lines start at one.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=101)** A negative numerical index can also be used to reference the opposite end of the grid.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=108)** A grid cell is a single unit defined by where the grid row and column intersect.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=113)** A grid track is basically the grid column or row.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=118)** Grid tracks can also be separated by a gutter.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=121)** Grid items can also be placed into grid areas.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=124)** It may span over one or more grid cells.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=127)** Unlike the grid track, this can span across rows and columns.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=132)** When using the grid layout columns and rows can be defined in adaptive ways by using explicit and implicit grids.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-css-grid?u=76281980&t=139)** This will be explored in the upcoming lessons.

#### [The explicit grid](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When using the [[CSS Grid Layout]], the number of grid tracks can be defined with two properties, grid template columns and grid template rows.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=9)** These properties are declared in the grid container and each value is separated by a space and represents the number of grid tracks.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=17)** This will create an explicit grid.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=21)** The grid layout also introduced a new flexible length unit, fr, which represents a fraction of the available space in the grid container.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=29)** For example, if grid template columns is set to 1fr three times, the grid will split the available space into three columns each with the same width.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=40)** If the value is set to 1fr, 2fr, 1fr, the grid items will still be arranged into three columns, however, the second track will take up twice the amount of space as the first and third tracks.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=53)** Values that are repeated can also be defined using the repeat function value.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=58)** Instead of writing 1fr three times, add the number of tracks you want to create within the parentheses, followed by a comma, then the size of the track.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=68)** The repeat function can be used with other value types.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=71)** In this example, the first column will have a fixed width of 50 pixels, and the following two columns will split the remaining space equally.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=79)** Let's put this together in an example.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=83)** The first step is to define the grid container by adding display grid to the parent element.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=89)** There won't be any visual changes just yet, but we can now apply the grid properties to the child elements.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=95)** Now let's add in the grid template columns property, each set to a width of 100 pixels.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=101)** Since this will create three columns, box four, five, and six will wrap onto a second row.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=109)** Adding grid template rows with two values of 100 pixels will create two rows that are 100 pixels in height.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=116)** Let's update the grid template columns value using the repeat function.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=122)** Add the number of columns in the parentheses then a comma and a width value.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=130)** The grid should look the same since we haven't changed the dimensions, just the value type.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=136)** Using the repeat function makes it easier to add or remove columns because you only have to change the first value.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=143)** Let's put it back to three columns.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=146)** But instead of using pixels for sizing, let's use the new flexible fr unit instead.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=152)** To create three equal columns relative to the size of the container, set the value to 1fr.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=160)** Now let's try using the repeat function with other values.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=164)** I can set the first column to a fixed size of 100 pixels.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=170)** Then use the repeat function to create two equal columns set to 1fr.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=177)** The remaining space will be split evenly between these two columns.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=181)** The fr unit is relative to the size of the grid container.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=185)** Right now, it's the same size as the view port.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=188)** But if we add a width to the grid container, the columns set with the fr unit will be sized accordingly.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=195)** So far we've been making changes using grid template columns but these value types and units can also be used with grid template rows.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=203)** Right now, there are two values indicating two rows with the height of 100 pixels, let's delete one of those values.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=212)** The second row is now the same height as its content instead of 100 pixels because we've only declared one explicit row value.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=221)** Any items that are within the grid container, but are placed outside of the explicit grid, will be placed into an implicit grid and will be sized according to those rules, which we'll discuss in the next lesson.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-explicit-grid?u=76281980&t=235)** We'll also continue with this pin, so keep it open.

#### [The implicit grid](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When using a grid layout, grid template rows and grid template columns are used to define a fixed number of tracks to form an explicit grid.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=8)** Once the explicit grid is filled, or if no explicit grid has been defined, the grid container will create an implicit grid.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=16)** If you have dynamic content such as a newsfeed, search results, or a list of comments, you don't always know ahead of time how many items you need to display.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=25)** In this scenario, using an implicit grid is a good option.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=29)** To define the size of implicit grid tracks, use the grid auto rows and grid auto columns properties.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=37)** They use the same values and syntax as grid template rows and grid template columns.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=43)** Let's continue with the pen from the previous lesson.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=46)** Here is where we left off.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=48)** We had six grid items, but only defined three columns and one row in the explicit grid.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=54)** So items four to six are placed in an implicit row.

>

>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=98)** We'll stick with one value for grid template rows to define the size for the first row which will be for the recent posts.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=106)** I'll change it to 200 pixels just to make it a little bit bigger.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=111)** Then for any number of older posts, we'll add grid auto rows to set the height for items that will be placed in the implicit row.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=120)** Let's set that value to 800 pixels.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=124)** Now, once the explicit row has been filled, the remaining items are placed into an implicit row.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=130)** Let's hide the last three items in the [[HTML]] panel and see what happens.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=137)** We won't see any empty tracks because the implicit grid values will only apply when there are items placed outside of the explicit grid.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=144)** Let's put those items back into the HTML and add three more items.

>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=157)** Now, any additional items outside of the first explicit row will automatically be added to the implicit grid.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=164)** If you don't know how many items your grid needs to display, you can create a whole grid using only an implicit grid.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-implicit-grid?u=76281980&t=172)** If you know the minimum amount of grid items, you can use an explicit and implicit grid together.

#### [Adding gutters with the gap property](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/adding-gutters-with-the-gap-property?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/adding-gutters-with-the-gap-property?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the [[CSS Grid Layout]], gutters can be added between grid items using the gap property.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/adding-gutters-with-the-gap-property?u=76281980&t=6)** One value adds the same amount of space between the rows and columns.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/adding-gutters-with-the-gap-property?u=76281980&t=10)** Using two values sets different gutter values for the rows and columns.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/adding-gutters-with-the-gap-property?u=76281980&t=15)** The first value will be applied to the rows, the second to the columns.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/adding-gutters-with-the-gap-property?u=76281980&t=19)** Gap can be used with any length units, a percentage, or the calc function, which lets you perform calculations when specifying the values.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/adding-gutters-with-the-gap-property?u=76281980&t=28)** Different types of values can be used within the calculations.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/adding-gutters-with-the-gap-property?u=76281980&t=32)** While the FR unit can be used for sizing other grid properties, it can't be used with gap.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/adding-gutters-with-the-gap-property?u=76281980&t=38)** The gap property is also shorthand for row gap and column gap.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/adding-gutters-with-the-gap-property?u=76281980&t=43)** Originally, grid layout defined the gap property as grid gap and the longhand properties as grid row gap and grid column gap.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/adding-gutters-with-the-gap-property?u=76281980&t=52)** This property was updated to be used for both Flexbox and grid layouts.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/adding-gutters-with-the-gap-property?u=76281980&t=58)** We've covered many of the foundational grid concepts but CSS grid is an extensive layout module and there's more to learn.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/adding-gutters-with-the-gap-property?u=76281980&t=66)** If you want to dive deeper into CSS grid and Flexbox, there are a variety of courses available in the [[LinkedIn]] Learning library.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/adding-gutters-with-the-gap-property?u=76281980&t=73)** Although we've just scratched the surface, we now have enough knowledge to begin incorporating grid into our course project.

> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=385)** Add the column-gap property and a colon.
>
> **[6:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=393)** For the value, let's try 10 pixels.
>
> **[6:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=397)** That added some space, but I'd like a little more.
>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=400)** I'll increase it to 20 pixels.
>
> **[6:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=404)** That looks much better to me.
>
> **[6:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=406)** For this layout, I chose a fixed gutter size because I don't need it to be relative to its container.
>
> **[6:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=411)** I always want it to be 20 pixels of space between the columns, regardless of the container size.
>
> **[6:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=417)** Also, remember, changes made in the dev tools don't affect the CSS file and will be reset when the browser is refreshed, so don't forget to add these styles to the CSS file.
>
> **[7:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=428)** Let's do that now.
>
> **[7:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=430)** We'll update the grid-template-column values to 300 pixels and 1fr.
>
> **[7:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=438)** Then we'll add the column-gap property with a value of 20 pixels.
>
> **[7:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=444)** Then save the file, and check to make sure that we added everything in.
>
> **[7:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=452)** I still have my dev tools open, so I can see in the CSS panel and grid inspector that the styles have been added.
>
> **[7:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=460)** In a previous project update, we floated the images in the project section.
>
> **[7:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=465)** The content on my page is shorter than the image, so it looks like a two-column layout, but if the content was longer than the image, the text would wrap underneath.
>
> **[7:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=475)** If you don't want the text to flow underneath, you can use this grid technique we just covered or flexbox, as we did in the footer exercise.
>
> **[8:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=484)** Typically, a combination of layout methods are used, and every project is unique.
>
> **[8:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=489)** Finding the best solution requires practice and experience.
>
> **[8:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-adding-columns-with-grid?u=76281980&t=492)** This is all part of the process.

### 6. Typography

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Typography for the web](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=0)** - There are many [[Typography]] related CSS properties that can be used to change the default styles.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=5)** Typography refers to the overall design and practice of organizing text to communicate and engage with the reader, and it starts with choosing a typeface.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=15)** A typeface refers to the design of a set of letters, numbers and symbols that share common design features.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=21)** A font refers to the specific instance of a typeface.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=24)** For the web and for use with various software programs, a font refers to the digital file that contains the information about the typeface, such as the specific weight styles and characteristics.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=36)** Though there are some differences between the terms font and typeface, they're often used interchangeably.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=42)** A font family is a collection of related fonts.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=46)** When choosing a font, we want to use something that's easy to read but also conveys certain feelings or an appropriate tone for the content.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=54)** Some typeface can evoke strong emotional responses.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=58)** For example, Comic Sans was designed to be a casual font and became popular for materials aimed at children due to its fun appearance.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=67)** It quickly spread to other uses, and despite its popularity, many people found it inappropriate for serious or formal information, leading to a ban Comic Sans movement.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=78)** While these responses will vary among individuals, certain typeface are generally considered better suited for conveying particular feelings or tones.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=86)** There are five categories of typefaces.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=89)** Script typefaces have a hand-lettered look and are thought to convey an elegant feel.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=94)** So they're often used for wedding invitations.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=97)** On the web, they're usually reserved for headings or short blocks of text, to add some embellishments, since they can be harder to read in small sizes, all caps or long blocks of texts.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=108)** Decorative typefaces are ornamental and tend to have big personalities.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=112)** So for the web, they are usually reserved for headings and decorative details as well.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=117)** The characters in monospace typefaces occupies the same amount of horizontal space, whether it's a narrow I or a wider O.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=125)** Monospace typefaces are often used for displaying code.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=129)** Serif typefaces are characterized by small decorative lines usually at the top or bottom of the letters.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=136)** Serif fonts tend to be viewed as more traditional and formal.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=139)** For example, Times New Roman is commonly used for legal documents.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=144)** Sans serif typefaces do not have decorative lines and are often thought of as contemporary and modern.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=150)** For the web, the recommendation used to be that sans serif created a better reading experience, especially for long blocks of text, because the absence of small decorative lines on the letters wouldn't interrupt the flow.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=162)** And serif typefaces were often used for shorter blocks of text, such as headings.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=167)** But over the years, there's been a rise in the usage of serif fonts for larger blocks of texts.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=172)** For example, Medium is a popular platform for reading and publishing essays and uses a serif typeface for the majority of the text on its platform.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/typography-for-the-web?u=76281980&t=181)** While design trends may change, it will always be necessary to ensure that your typeface choices are easy to read and convey the tone that you're aiming for.

#### [Changing fonts with font-family](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In CSS, the font-family property is used to define the typeface of an element.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=5)** The value is declared as a list, also known as a font stack.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=10)** It contains multiple font-family options, arranged by priority from highest to lowest.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=16)** The browser displays the first font in the list.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=18)** If it's not installed on the user's computer, it will try the next option until it finds an available font.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=25)** The font stack should include similar fallback options and always includes a generic font family as the last option.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=33)** Generic font families are defined by the browser and the exact font will vary across devices and operating systems.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=40)** Also, they don't refer to specific typefaces but rather, categories of fonts with similar characteristics.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=48)** The available keyword values for generic font families are serif, sans-serif, and monospace, which are used as fallbacks for the corresponding category of typefaces.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=58)** cursive is usually used as a fallback for script or decorative fonts.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=63)** fantasy is generally used for more unconventional decorative fonts.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=67)** The system-ui keyword matches to the default operating systems font, which is usually a sans-serif typeface.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=74)** For example, Macs use the San Francisco font and [[Windows]] uses Segoe UI.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=81)** It can also be used as the first option in a font stack to integrate the design with the look and feel of the operating system the user is viewing the website on.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=91)** Although system-ui maps to the default font of the OS, you can still include specific font-family options in the font stack.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=98)** There's no limit to the number of fonts that can be included in the stack, but you should define at least your preferred font followed by a generic font.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=107)** One more thing to note about font-family.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=109)** If the name contains spaces, numbers, or characters other than a hyphen, they should be contained in single or double quotes.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=117)** Generic fonts, however, must always be unquoted.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=122)** Using a font stack provides fallback options, but how do we know what fonts are available?
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=127)** In the past, font choices were limited since we have no way of knowing what fonts are installed on the user's computer.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=133)** But there are some known fonts that are generally available on most operating systems.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=139)** These are known as web-safe fonts.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=142)** [cssfontstack.com](https://cssfontstack.com) has a list of web-safe fonts categorized by typeface and shows the availability of the font on Windows and Macs.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=151)** There's no 100% guarantee that these fonts will be available on the user's computer since they could also delete fonts from their system too, but that's why we use fallbacks.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=163)** Modern Font Stacks list various font stacks that organizes typeface options by category and operating system.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/changing-fonts-with-font-family?u=76281980&t=173)** There are other ways to expand our font options using web fonts, which we'll discuss in an upcoming lesson.

#### [font-weight and font-style](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Font families often include different fonts with varying amounts of thickness, which determines how thin or bold the characters appear.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=7)** This is defined by the font weight property which uses numeric keyword values.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=12)** 100 represents the lightest or thinnest font weight.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=15)** The values go up in increments of 100 to a maximum of 900, which represents the darkest or thickest font.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=23)** These numbers roughly correspond to commonly-used weight names like thin or extra light.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=28)** These weight names are not CSS keyword values though but there are some non-numeric keyword values that can be used.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=35)** Normal is equal to the numeric value of 400 and is also the default for body text.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=42)** Bold is equal to 700 and is the default for headings.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=45)** Not all font families will include all nine weights.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=50)** If you use a value to specify a weight for which no font file exists, the typeface with the nearest weight is used.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=56)** In this example, if you set your font weight to 600, it will default to the font with the 700 weight.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=64)** Some font families will also include an italic style which can be specified with the font style property.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=70)** There are three values, italic, oblique, and normal.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=75)** An italic style is created by the type designer to not only appear slanted, but are often drawn in a more slightly cursive style and is influenced by calligraphy.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=85)** An oblique style is basically a slanted version of the regular font.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=90)** If there's no italic font the italic value will default to the oblique font.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=96)** If there's no oblique font, then the browser will simulate the italic effect by slanting the regular font.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=103)** The same is true for the oblique value, but it will try to map to an oblique font first.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=109)** The normal value maps to the normal font and is usually used to remove a default italic or oblique style.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=115)** Let's try this out in a [[CodePen]] example.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=118)** Both of these properties are inheritable so I can add font weight bold to the header selector and all the nested elements will be bolded.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=126)** The H1 looks the same because it's already bolded by default, but the default style can be changed by selecting the H1 directly.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=135)** Setting the font weight to 400 will remove the default bold style.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=141)** For the font style property, let's use the M selector to remove the default italic style from this element by setting it to normal.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=151)** Back in the header selector, we can add the italic font style to apply this to all the nested elements.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=157)** If we use the oblique value instead, it doesn't seem to change, so we can assume that there's no specific italic font in this font family.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=166)** This is another example of separating content from style.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-weight-and-font-style?u=76281980&t=170)** For example, use the proper heading tags to create a hierarchy within the content rather than making an element bold or a specific size, then use CSS properties to add or remove these styles.

#### [Web fonts with @font-face](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=0)** - [[Typography]] is an important part of [[Web Design]] and in the past, the options of available fonts were limited to a small selection of web-safe fonts.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=9)** However, with the introduction of the CSS @font-face rule, our options have expanded to include web fonts.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=17)** This means that instead of relying on fonts installed on a user's computer, font files can now be downloaded along with your website just like any other resource, such as images or PDFs.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=28)** Web fonts are added using the @font-face rule.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=32)** The syntax looks similar to the CSS declarations we've been writing so far, but we're not selecting or applying styles to any elements.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=40)** Instead, we're writing instructions.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=43)** Let's take a closer look at each part.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=46)** Start the declaration with @font-face and the curly brackets.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=50)** This must be declared in your style sheet before you can use your font, then add font-family.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=56)** In this context, it's used to define the name of the font family.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=60)** You can name it whatever you want but it's convention to use the same name as the typeface.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=66)** The source property is used to include the path to the font files with a functional URL value.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=73)** It can be a relative or absolute path, just like linking to any other files such as images or a CSS file.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=80)** Most modern browsers support the WOFF and WOFF2 font formats, and both are typically listed.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=86)** When using multiple font files add a comma between each URL value.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=92)** The format function is an optimal declaration that could be added after the URL value to provide information about the font format to the browser.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=101)** Once these values are defined you're ready to start using your web font.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=105)** The font family name specified inside the @font-face declaration is used as the value for the font family property in the same way as web safe fonts are used.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=116)** The WOFF and WOFF2 formats are supported by modern browsers but if you need to support older browsers, different formats are required.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=125)** If you don't have these different file types [fontsquirrel.com](https://fontsquirrel.com) has a free font generator that can create different file types.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=133)** Also, note that when downloading a font, even if it's free, there are often usage guidelines.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=138)** Be sure to review any read me files or licensing info before using it.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/web-fonts-with-font-face?u=76281980&t=144)** To learn more about @font-face and support for older browsers, check out this article by CSS-Tricks.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Typography]] (1), [[Web Design]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** css (4), url (3), woff (2), woff2 (2)
> **Analogies:** just like (2), such as (2), similar to (1)
> **Definitions:** is an  (2), means that (1)
> **URLs:** [fontsquirrel.com](https://fontsquirrel.com) (1)
> **Warnings:** note that (1)

#### [The font-size property](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The font-size property defines the size of [[HTML]] texts and can be used with a variety of value types.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=7)** In this lesson, we'll focus on three commonly used types, pixel, em and rem.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=13)** Using pixels for sizing is an ideal unit for accuracy since pixels are an absolute value, meaning they're fixed and not affected by font sizing styles in ancestor elements.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=25)** Also, stick to using whole number values since different browsers interpret decimal values inconsistently with some rounding up and others rounding down.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=34)** The default size of the base text in an HTML document is equivalent to 16 pixels with the exception of headings.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=42)** Headings are bigger or smaller depending on the heading level.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=46)** The em unit is named after the letter M, which was historically used in [[Typography]] to measure widths.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=53)** In CSS, em is a relative unit, sized in relation to its closest ancestor element, font-size.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=61)** 1em is equal to its inherited font size.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=65)** For example, a value of 1.5 will be one and a half times the size of the inherited font size.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=72)** If no font size is declared anywhere, then em will be sized relative to the default browser font size.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=79)** Unlike pixels, it's common to use decimal values with the em unit to calculate more precise font sizes.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=87)** Rem stands for root em.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=89)** It's a relative unit as well, and is similar to the em unit but it's only relative to the root element, which is the HTML element.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=98)** Font sizing added to any other ancestor elements won't affect the rem unit.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=105)** Let's open a [[CodePen]] example.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=108)** The child element is currently displayed with the default text size of 16 pixels.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=113)** If I add a font-size of 13m, it will look the same because 1em is equal to the default.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=121)** Let's change it to 2em.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=126)** Now it's twice the size, making it equivalent to 32 pixels.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=131)** If a font-size is declared in a parent or ancestor element, the child element will be sized relative to that value.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=139)** So if I set the parent to 2em, the child element is now twice the size of the parent element, which is also twice the size of the default value of 16 pixels.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=153)** When using em, it's common to use decimal values to define more precise values.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=159)** So let's change the child element's font-size to .5em.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=166)** This makes it half the size of the parent element.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=170)** There's no limit to the number of decimal points we can use.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=173)** So if we set this value to 0.575, that will be valid too.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=179)** The em unit can also be sized relative to other units.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=183)** For example, if I use pixels in the parent element, em will be relative to that value.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=189)** Let's change this to 60 pixels.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=195)** Now let's take a look at how rem works.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=198)** Change the value in the child element to 1rem.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=204)** Now the font-size is equivalent to 16 pixels again since rem is not affected by the size of the parent or ancestor elements.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=213)** Let's add the font-size to the root element using the HTML selector.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=220)** Now the child element will be resized because it's relative only to the root element.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=226)** You can also mix units with rem as well.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=229)** If I were to change this to 30 pixels, 1rem will equal 30 pixels.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=236)** Using relative values can be tricky at first but they're useful for accessibility and for creating responsive styles.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-font-size-property?u=76281980&t=243)** We'll discuss this in more detail in the next lesson.
