#### [Font-size and accessibility](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Setting font sizes in pixels is a common practice, but using relative sizing is often a better choice for accessibility.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=8)** I'm going to demonstrate how relative font sizing works in the browser using the example [[HTML]] file from exercise 01_03.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=17)** I'm using Firefox, but this will apply to other browsers as well.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=22)** This is how the default font size of the text looks before we add any CSS.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=28)** As mentioned before, the text is equal to 16 pixels except for the headings, which range in size according to the heading level.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=37)** In the browser settings under fonts, we can confirm that the default size is 16 pixels.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=43)** I'll change it to 20, then refresh the browser tab containing the HTML file.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=52)** With this setting updated, the default font size for the browser is now equal to 20 pixels.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=58)** The heading text is also larger, but maintains the same sizing hierarchy.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=63)** This is because default browser styles are defined using the relative M unit.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=69)** By opening the dev tools and inspecting the H1 heading, the user agent style, which is the browser default style, is set to 2M.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=79)** This will make the H1 twice as big as the default font size.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=84)** The [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of the headings are sized with M as well.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=86)** The H2 is 1.5 M, and the sizes get progressively smaller the lower you go down with the heading levels.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=98)** For paragraphs and lists, there is no user agent font size set, which makes it equal to the browser settings.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=104)** So to recap, when the browser font size is set to the default paragraphs and lists are 16 pixels and headings are sized relative to that value.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=113)** Changing the default 20 pixels increases the font size of paragraphs and lists to 20 pixels, and headings are sized relative to the new default value.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=124)** Let's see what happens when I add a font size value.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=127)** Using pixels, I'll use the dev tools so I can make the change right in the browser.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=133)** Let's say I want my base font size to be 14 pixels.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=136)** I'll select the body element and add a font size of 14 pixels.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=149)** Now the text is sized according to the font size CSS I added to the body element and ignores the user settings.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=158)** But if I used 0.875 rem instead, for those who have the default set to 16 pixels, this base text will be equal to 14 pixels, but my browser is still set to 20 pixels.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=172)** So using this rem value will still reduce the font size, but it will be relative to the user's browser settings.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=182)** Being able to change font size preferences is especially important for people with vision impairments, so let's talk about how to use font sizing in a way that allows us to maintain control over our designs while respecting the preferences of our website visitors.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=197)** For the font size property, we talked about using two relative units, em and rem.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=203)** It's generally easier to maintain consistency using rem since it's only relative to the root element.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=210)** Even though the browser default styles uses em, the sizes we declare in our CSS files will override the defaults.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=219)** If you're building a website from a mock-up, it's commonly created using software like Photoshop and the dimensions are often set to pixels.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=227)** For CSS, we need to convert them to rem.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=231)** To do the conversion, divide the desired pixel value by the default root font size to get the rem value.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=239)** Though users can change the browser font size settings, we can use 16 as the root size for the calculations since it's the most common setting.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=248)** So 16 pixels equals one rem, 32 pixels equals two rem, and eight pixels is 0.5 rem.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=257)** That's not too hard to calculate, but other font sizes don't divide quite as neatly.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=263)** For example, the 0.875 rem value I just used in the browser demo is equal to 14 pixels, but there's no limit to how many decimal values can be used with rem, so these calculations work just fine, but there is another option for those who wouldn't mind doing a little less math.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=284)** The 62.5 method was popularized by designer Richard Rutter in an article stemming back to 2004.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=291)** He presents the idea for setting the root font size to 62.5% which takes the default value of 16 pixels down to 10.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=301)** Now, the math is easier because the desired pixel amount is divided by 10.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=306)** So 10 pixels equals one rem.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=309)** 14 pixels is 1.4 rem, 15 is 1.5 rem, and so on.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=316)** To apply this method to a project, set the root element using the HTML selector to 62.5%, then set your desired base font size to the body element using rem.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=328)** If you leave it like this, the heading elements will scale according to the body's font size, and the rest of the text will be sized to 16 pixels.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=338)** But most of the time, you'll want to adjust the font size of specific elements, which you can do by using more specific selectors.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=346)** Now, you might be thinking, why did I bring the font size down in the root element just to bring it back up again in the body element?
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=353)** This just makes the default font size equivalent to 16 pixels again.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=358)** Well, the 62.5 method just simplifies the math.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=362)** If you don't use it, stick to the original calculation, the desired pixel amount divided by 16, and if you're not working off any specific design dimensions,.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=372)** choose whatever font size values look good to you.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=376)** How you decide to implement relative sizing is your personal preference.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=380)** To dive deeper into this topic, check out this article.
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=384)** Basically, what this boils down to is relative sizing allows you to set font sizes without overriding user preferences.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/font-size-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=392)** This doesn't mean we should never use pixels, but it's important to consider a more flexible approach when possible.

#### [Text-align and text-transform](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's talk about the text-align and text-transform properties.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=4)** The text-align property is an inheritable value and is used to align content contained within a block element.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=11)** It's defined using various keyword values.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=15)** The text-transform property is used to control the capitalization of the text in an element.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=21)** It's also an inheritable style and used with various keyword values.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=26)** Let's see how these values are applied in a [[CodePen]] example.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=30)** For text-align, the keyword, center, is used to center align the text.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=34)** Let's add this style to the h1 selector.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=39)** It's an inheritable style, so if I add it to the parent element using the section selector, all the child elements will also be aligned.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=48)** Even though this property is called text-align, this style is also applied to image elements.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=55)** Continuing with the section selector, let's add the left value.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=63)** This is the default value, and not surprisingly, it aligns the content to the left edge.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=68)** We had applied text-align center specifically to the h1 element.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=72)** That's why that heading isn't aligned to the left like all the other child elements.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=78)** Specific styles override inherited styles.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=82)** The start value is similar to left, but it's related to the direction of the text based on the language.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=89)** So if it's a left-to-right language, start refers to the left side.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=93)** So we won't see a change when adding this style in to this example, but if it's a right-to-left language, then start refers to the right side.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=102)** And the opposite is true for both the right and end values.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=114)** justify adds spacing between the words to evenly space the text to the left and right of the containing element except for the last line.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=124)** This style is often used to make narrow columns of text resemble a newspaper, but it's not commonly used on websites because it can be hard to read for longer blocks of text.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=135)** One thing to note about the text-align property, when you apply it to a specific element, it only works on a block element or elements using the display property set to block or inline block.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=149)** Inline elements are the same size as their content.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=153)** I'll put a background color on the span element to demonstrate.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=158)** text-align arranges the content within the element, so if the element is the same size as its content, there's no space to move it in any direction to change the alignment.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=169)** Now let's take a look at text-transform and add the uppercase value.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=177)** As we can see, this is also an inheritable style, uppercase converts all the characters to uppercase.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=186)** The lowercase value will convert all the characters to lowercase, and capitalize will convert only the first letter of each [[Microsoft Word|word]] to uppercase.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=197)** We're going to be exploring a couple more text-related styles, so keep this CodePen example open.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/text-align-and-text-transform?u=76281980&t=204)** We'll be using it again.

#### [Line-height and letter-spacing](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's take a look at two more text-based CSS properties, line height and letter spacing.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=6)** Line height sets the vertical spacing between lines of text.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=10)** It can be used with different value types, including length, number, percentage, and the keyword normal.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=17)** The letter spacing property sets the horizontal spacing between text characters.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=22)** It can be defined with length values and the keyword normal.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=26)** Let's go back to the code pin from the previous lesson.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=30)** Line height is related to font size, which right now is displaying the default value of 16 pixels.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=38)** I'll start by commenting out the styles from the previous exercise.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=44)** Now, let's add line height to the P element.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=49)** If the value is the same as the font size, there will be no space between the lines.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=55)** If the value is smaller, then the lines will overlap.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=60)** To add space between the lines, choose a value that is larger than the font size which will make it easier to read.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=67)** I'll set it to 20 pixels.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=70)** Though you can use pixels, it's an absolute unit, so if I add in the font size of 20 pixels, the line height will remain the same.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=79)** To make the line height more flexible, use a relative unit.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=83)** If I set the value to 150%, it will be 150% of whatever the font size is.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=91)** So if I change the font size, the line height will be relative to the new value.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=97)** My preferred method is to use a unit list number value.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=100)** Setting it to 1.5 would be the same as 150%, but personally, this just makes more sense to me.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=109)** The default value of line height is the keyword normal and the exact value depends on the browser and the font family, but it's roughly equal to 1.2.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=119)** So if I change this to 1.2, then comment it out, you can see that it only shifts slightly.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=128)** I tend to use a value between 1.25 and 1.5, depending on the font size and font family.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=138)** Now let's move on to the letter spacing property and add it to the h1 element.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=147)** Adding a positive value will space the characters farther apart.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=152)** Negative values will bring them closer together.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=156)** Most of the time, the default letter spacing doesn't need to be adjusted, but if it does, it's usually just a bit of extra space.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=163)** So I prefer to use the M unit, then I can use a decimal to allow for a more precise value.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=170)** Let's try setting it to 0.05 M.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=176)** For letter spacing, there really isn't one value that works for all font families.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=180)** This will vary depending on the width of the characters, as well as the font size.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=185)** There are more font related properties, and some are more commonly used than others.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/line-height-and-letter-spacing?u=76281980&t=189)** That's why it's good to have reliable resources to reference, whether you've been writing CSS for 10 days or 10 years.

> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=120)** Usually you only have to add a little bit of space, so as you can see here, 1em is way too much space, so let's bring it down to 0.1 instead.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=132)** Now, here's how it looks with the letter spacing and how it looks without the letter spacing.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=138)** I think 0.1 adds just enough space.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=142)** Also, notice that I'm using em here instead of rem.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=146)** This is to make the sizing relative to the font size of the current element, which is the button, rather than the font size of the root element.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=155)** Now for the padding, we had added a value of seven pixels, which should have added the same amount around all four sides, but it looks like there's a little bit more on the top and the bottom, and that's because of the line height again.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=169)** So let's set line height to one.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=177)** Now, to make rounded corners, we'll use the border-radius property.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=184)** We can use a number of value here, so I'm going to go with four pixels to make it slightly rounded.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=192)** You can also use the arrow keys to change the value by increments of one.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=198)** The higher the number, the rounder the corners.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=202)** I only want it to be slightly rounded, so I think I'll just stick with four pixels.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=207)** If you're happy with the values, let's go back to the CSS file and add them.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=213)** So we added a letter spacing value of 0.1em, and we also
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=222)** set the line height to one, and then added a border radius value of four pixels.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=234)** All right, so let's save the file and just double check that we've captured all the changes in the browser.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=242)** All right, so the updated button styles have been applied, but now I want to make a change to just the Download PDF button, which uses the same button styles.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=252)** Let's differentiate this by making the text bigger.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=256)** Back in the CSS file, we already created a second class for styling the download link under the Resume section.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=264)** Let's update the font size here.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=267)** I'll go with 1.5rem.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=271)** Let's check these updates.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=275)** Now the button size is bigger.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=277)** Let's scroll down to the work experience area.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=281)** We just need to adjust a little spacing.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=284)** For example, I'd like to remove the margins from the paragraphs in the first column.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=290)** That way all these work details can be closer together.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=294)** Same with the Education section.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=297)** For the summary in the work items, I just want to remove the margin from the top of the first paragraph so that it can line up with the text in the first column, but I want to keep the space in the following paragraphs.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=313)** So let's go back to the editor and add these updates.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=318)** Looking at the [[HTML]] structure for the work items, the paragraphs in the left column is contained in the work details div.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=328)** We'll remove all the margins from these paragraphs.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=332)** In the Work Summary column, we just want to remove the top margin of the first paragraph.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=338)** In the CSS file, let's add a new declaration block for these paragraphs.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=344)** Under the Work section, we'll start with the Work Details class, then add a space and P to select the paragraphs, and we'll set the margin to zero.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=358)** Then for the work summary, we'll select just the first paragraph.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=364)** We'll start with a work summary space P, and then we're going to use a pseudo selector.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=372)** Way back in chapter two, we talked about using the first child pseudo selector to target the first element in a section.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=380)** Let's use that now.
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=382)** Add a colon first-child, we'll add our curly brackets, and then set the margin just for the top to zero.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=392)** Now let's go back to the HTML file and create a new class for the Education section.
>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=400)** Each item is contained within its own section element, so let's add a class to each of these blocks.
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=407)** I'll call it education item.
>
> **[6:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=412)** Then I'll copy this class and add it to the other items.
>
> **[6:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=418)** We'll be targeting the paragraphs here as well.
>
> **[7:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=421)** So let's switch over to the CSS file and add the new selector.
>
> **[7:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=426)** So we'll start this with the education item class space P, and set the margin to zero.
>
> **[7:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=440)** Okay, we've added a bunch of styles, so let's save the file and check the changes in the browser.
>
> **[7:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=447)** In the Work Experience and Education section, the text is now all lined up, but we still need to add one more thing.

>

>
> **[7:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=464)** This time, we'll add margin to the top and bottom of each work item and education item.
>
> **[7:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=474)** I'm already here in the Education section, so let's add that one first.
>
> **[8:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=480)** We'll add the education item class, and I'll set the margin to 30 pixels on the top and bottom, and zero on the left and right, and let's do the same for the work item.
>
> **[8:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=498)** A margin of 30 pixels again, to the top and bottom, and zero on the left and right, and let's save the file and check the update.
>
> **[8:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=510)** Now we can see the spaces in between each item in both the Work Experience and Education sections.
>
> **[8:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=518)** Although it may seem counterintuitive to remove default styles and then add in our own, it's necessary.
>
> **[8:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=525)** Remember, HTML was originally meant for sharing text-based documents, but it's now used for much more, so we need to adjust the styles accordingly.
>
> **[8:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=534)** Some developers use a reset style sheet to remove default styles and start fresh.
>
> **[9:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=541)** This article discusses the reasoning history and options.
>
> **[9:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=544)** I personally don't use reset style sheets, and when you're learning, it's good to see what the default styles look like so you know how to deal with them.
>
> **[9:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=552)** Using a reset is another one of those widely debated developer topics, and so there's no right or wrong answer.
>
> **[9:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-buttons-and-spacing?u=76281980&t=558)** Check out the article and decide if it makes sense for you.

### 7. Layouts: Fluid and Responsive

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Intro to responsive design](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-responsive-design?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-responsive-design?u=76281980&t=0)** - When I started building websites back in 2006, we didn't have to worry too much about different screen sizes.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-responsive-design?u=76281980&t=6)** Most people were still browsing websites on desktops and laptops, but there were a couple of major developments around that time that started the push towards more widespread mobile browsing.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-responsive-design?u=76281980&t=17)** The Opera Mini browser was introduced in 2005 with the hopes of finally bringing mobile internet into the mainstream.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-responsive-design?u=76281980&t=25)** The first iPhone was introduced in 2007, which took mobile browsing to the next level by including a touch screen.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-responsive-design?u=76281980&t=33)** Companies were starting to realize they needed to optimize for smaller screens.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-responsive-design?u=76281980&t=37)** In 2010, Ethan Marcotte coined the term [[Responsive Web Design]] in his article for the publication, "A list apart."
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-responsive-design?u=76281980&t=45)** In the article he said, "Fluid grids, flexible images, and media queries are the three technical ingredients for responsive web design."
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-responsive-design?u=76281980&t=54)** But it also requires a different way of thinking.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-responsive-design?u=76281980&t=58)** Rather than quarantining our content into device specific experiences, we can use media queries to progressively enhance our work within different viewing contexts.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-responsive-design?u=76281980&t=69)** This way of designing was once considered an extra feature, but these days, we can access the web on many different devices.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-responsive-design?u=76281980&t=76)** So responsive web design can no longer be an afterthought.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-responsive-design?u=76281980&t=80)** By creating websites that work seamlessly on any device, we can improve the [[User Experience (UX)|user experience]] and make our websites accessible to a wider audience.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Responsive Web Design]] (3), [[User Experience (UX)|User experience]] (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** iphone (1)
> **Speakers:** - when (1)

#### [Intro to media queries](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Media queries make it possible to selectively apply CSS based on specific conditions, such as different screen sizes or device orientation.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=10)** Let's break down the syntax.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=12)** Media queries start with @media, which is one of many CSS at-rules.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=18)** We've talked about another one previously, at-font face.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=21)** At-rules look similar to CSS declarations, but they are statements used to instruct CSS how to behave.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=29)** The next part is the media type, which defines the broad category of device that is being used to display the website.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=36)** There are three types.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=39)** Print matches to printers and related displays like a Print Preview page shown in the browser.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=45)** This media type would be used for creating style specific for printing.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=49)** For example, removing the background color would be helpful so that someone who wants to print the page won't use up all their ink.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=56)** Screen matches to devices with a screen.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=60)** For responsive CSS, this media type is generally used, and all matches to all devices.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=67)** If the media type is not explicitly defined, it will default to all.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=74)** In the previous specification, there were more specific media types, but are now recommended by the W3C to no longer use.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=82)** Instead, media features provide additional ways to test for specific features of the browser or device, such as resolution or device orientation.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=93)** Media features look similar to CSS properties since they consist of a name, colon, and value, but the syntax is a bit different.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=101)** They're always wrapped in parentheses and there is no semicolon.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=107)** There are many media features available.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=109)** For instance, you can test for a specific width to height aspect ratio of the Viewport.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=116)** You can also test for different color features, height, orientation, and more.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=123)** They can all be found listed on the MDN documentation.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=128)** Responsive styles typically adjust to different screen sizes by targeting the width of the Viewport.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=134)** The Width Media feature is used to test the exact width of the Viewport.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=139)** efore responsive styles, it's usually more useful to use a range by using min-width or max-width media types.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=146)** This will be our focus for the upcoming project updates.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=151)** If you're using a media type with a media feature, the conditions are combined with logical operators.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=157)** The key [[Microsoft Word|word]] "and" combines the type and features into one query.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=163)** You can also create more complex queries using "and" to combine multiple media features.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=170)** There are more logical operators as well, such as "not," "only," "comma," "and/or," but this level of complexity is more than what we need for this course, but it's all documented in the MDN Web Docs if you'd like to learn more.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=188)** After defining the conditions for the query, the CSS to be applied is written just like any other CSS styles.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=195)** It just needs to be contained within the curly brackets of the media query.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=200)** All of these conditions must be true before the CSS is applied.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=204)** This example translates to, when the browser Viewport is smaller than 650 pixels, apply the CSS contained within the query.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=214)** If this condition is not met, the CSS is ignored.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=218)** So now that we know how to write a media query, the next question is, where do we write the media query?
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=225)** One option is to add it below the related styles.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=229)** Another option is to group all the media queries together and add it to the bottom of the CSS file.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=237)** The media queries can also be written in its own separate style sheet.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=241)** Let's say you want to create one just for mobile styles.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=244)** The style sheet is loaded into the [[HTML]] file with the link tag, just like any other CSS file, but a media attribute must also be added.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=254)** The media type and feature is added as the media value.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=259)** This option may be more useful when working with a large project with a lot of CSS.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/intro-to-media-queries?u=76281980&t=265)** Since the course project is a smaller project, we'll be adding the media queries to our existing CSS file.

#### [Breakpoints and media queries](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Media queries are used to selectively apply CSS based on specific conditions.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=5)** A breakpoint is the point where the media query is introduced to make these changes.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=11)** In [[Responsive Web Design]], this is often a specific width of a screen or browser view port.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=18)** In the early days of responsive design, it was common to use two to four breakpoints to target the width of specific device types, mobile, tablet, laptops, and desktops.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=30)** But with the rise of the number of devices available, it's harder to pinpoint what is considered a common size.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=39)** StatCounter is an analytics tool that provides various types of website user stats.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=44)** Here are the screen resolution stats for the period of March 2022 to March 2023.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=51)** The defaults show worldwide usage, but you can filter by region as well.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=57)** Right now it's also showing all platforms, but you can filter by type to check specific device categories.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=63)** I already have it open in my browser, since it sometimes takes a few minutes to load the chart.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=69)** Here are the desktop stats with a bunch of different sizes as well as a large chunk categorized as other.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=76)** We can see all the different resolutions on the left side of the bar graph.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=82)** Here's the stats for tablets.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=84)** As we can see, there's also various sizes here as well.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=89)** Same with mobile screens.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=93)** The most common media feature used for breakpoints is still width.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=97)** Except these days it's better to choose breakpoints where the design and layout starts to break, rather than focusing on specific device sizes.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=107)** The width media feature targets the width of the browser viewport.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=111)** We can also use a min or max prefix to specify a minimum or maximum value.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=118)** The width value is true when the viewport is exactly equal to the defined value.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=123)** So using min or max width is better because it specifies a range such as larger than or smaller than.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=131)** But when using min and max values, there's a possibility for conflict if the same breakpoint is used, since both range values include the value itself.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=141)** To prevent both queries from being true at the same time, one of the breakpoints can be set to be one pixel smaller or larger.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=150)** So how do you know when to use min width versus max width?
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=154)** Well, there are two schools of thought when designing for different screens.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=159)** One is desktop first, meaning you design and write your CSS for bigger screens first.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=165)** Media queries are then used to make changes for smaller screens.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=169)** This approach usually uses max-width media queries.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=173)** A mobile-first approach is the opposite.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=176)** The designs in CSS are optimized for smaller sizes first.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=180)** Then media queries are used to make changes for bigger screens.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=184)** This approach usually uses min-width media queries.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=189)** You can also combine both min and max to create a range.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=192)** In this example, the media query only applies to a width between 400 and 800 pixels, nothing bigger or smaller.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=202)** There are no limits to the number of breakpoints that can be added.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/breakpoints-and-media-queries?u=76281980&t=205)** However, as with writing CSS in general, it's recommended to use only what you need.

#### [Fluid layouts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've talked about how to use media queries to make changes for any viewport width, but do we always need to use a media query?
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=8)** Remember, there are three ingredients for [[Responsive Web Design]]: fluid layouts, flexible images, and media queries.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=17)** Building your website on a flexible foundation first means you don't need to use media queries to target every possible size.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=25)** Let's go over some techniques for creating a fluid layout, also referred to as a liquid layout.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=31)** When setting the width of the page components, use percentage values or relative units where possible.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=38)** Another option is to use the min and max-width properties which is the technique we applied to our content wrappers.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=46)** In this scenario, using max-width allowed us to have precise control over how wide the content wrappers spanned on larger screens.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=56)** Because we don't want it to get any larger than the maximum value on any screen, it makes sense here to use a pixel value, but for smaller screens, it's still flexible.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=65)** Any viewport size smaller than the max-width spans the full width.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=70)** So while we may need to make adjustments to other parts of the page when the content doesn't fit anymore, the content wrapper fits and flexes as needed for small and large screens.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=81)** Using Flexbox and Grid will also ensure that your layouts are fluid since it's pretty much built into both of these layout modules.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=89)** In the course project, we used the flex property to create the sticky footer.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=94)** Since the height of the browser is not fixed, using flex allows the content to stretch relative to the size of the browser viewport at any given size in order to keep the footer aligned to the bottom of the page.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=108)** We also used Grid with the fr unit to create flexible columns in the Work Experience section.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=114)** Even though grid template columns was paired with a fixed width, the second column is still fluid and sized relative to the available space in the container.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=124)** Just like page layouts, content containers, and other components on a webpage, setting your images to be flexible is another important part of creating a fluid layout.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=134)** We've applied this to the background images in the course project with the use of background size set to cover.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=141)** This makes it relative to the viewport.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/fluid-layouts?u=76281980&t=144)** While fluid layouts may not solve everything, starting with flexible CSS will set the foundation for optimizing layouts for different screen sizes rather than relying solely on media queries.

#### [The viewport meta tag](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When mobile browsers first came into use, websites often used fixed-width layouts that were designed for desktop and laptop computers.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=9)** Since phone screens are much smaller, loading the desktop sized layout on a webpage would result in a lot of content being cut off.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=17)** By default, mobile browsers would scale down the websites to fit the device screen, presenting a zoomed out version of the desktop layout.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=26)** With touchscreens, users can pinch and [[Zoom]] in and out of different parts of the website.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=32)** Mobile browsers still use this default behavior today.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=36)** When adding responsive styles and media queries, we need to disable the scaling so your mobile optimized styles display as intended.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=45)** We can do that using an [[HTML]] tag, often referred to as the viewport meta tag because it provides the browser with information about the viewport.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=56)** The HTML template for the course project already includes this tag in the head section, so there's no need to add it again, but for reference, this is what it looks like.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=66)** Let's go over the syntax.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=68)** Meta tags can be used in a variety of ways, but for this purpose, the name attribute is set to viewport.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=75)** So viewport specific information can be added into the content attribute.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=80)** The width=device-width value sets the width of the page to the same width as the device.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=88)** initial-scale=one means that the page won't appear zoomed in or out on page load.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=95)** While users can still zoom in or out to view different parts of the page after it's loaded, in general, you want to optimize your websites so users can just view the page as is.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/the-viewport-meta-tag?u=76281980&t=106)** So when adding responsive CSS styles you should always include the viewport meta tag.

#### [Project: Breakpoints](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-breakpoints?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-breakpoints?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For this exercise, let's use the developer tools to test the current project layout and help us decide what break points to use.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-breakpoints?u=76281980&t=10)** If you don't already have the dev tools open, go ahead and open it now.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-breakpoints?u=76281980&t=15)** All right, let's do our first test, starting with the homepage.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-breakpoints?u=76281980&t=19)** Rather than resizing the view port, I'm going to use the device emulator so I can resize that instead of the dev tools panel.
>

>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-breakpoints?u=76281980&t=166)** So for my longest block of text, it looks like around the 860 mark is about as small as I can go.

>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-breakpoints?u=76281980&t=187)** So I may as well just use the 860 pixel breakpoint as well.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-breakpoints?u=76281980&t=191)** Depending on how much content you have, your values may be different.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-breakpoints?u=76281980&t=195)** Plus it doesn't have to be exact.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-breakpoints?u=76281980&t=198)** If you find one break point at 860 and another at 900, then you can just use 900.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-breakpoints?u=76281980&t=203)** There's no limit to how many break points you can have, but adding too many can make your code more difficult to maintain.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-breakpoints?u=76281980&t=210)** It may also lead to performance issues.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-breakpoints?u=76281980&t=213)** As you probably already know by now, only include what you need.

> **[9:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-1?u=76281980&t=594)** Save the file and go back to the browser.
>
> **[10:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-1?u=76281980&t=602)** So now when the viewport is under 500 pixels, the thumbnail will span the width of the viewport.

>
> **[10:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-1?u=76281980&t=623)** Once we hit 650, we go back to two columns and then back up to the floated image at 860.
>
> **[10:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-1?u=76281980&t=633)** Let's make one more update to the download PDF button, since it's fixed on top of the page content.
>

> **[10:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-1?u=76281980&t=646)** I'm going to close the DevTools so I can make this viewport wider and see what size the button can remain fixed.

> **[11:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-1?u=76281980&t=668)** So let's go back to the CSS file and add a media query for this style.

> **[11:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-1?u=76281980&t=674)** So under the original download style link, I'll use max width to set the position.

>
> **[11:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-1?u=76281980&t=693)** And then we will use the download link selector again.

> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=110)** So now let's see at what size the footer needs to be adjusted.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=117)** So around 675 up to our next breakpoint, 860.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=127)** So we could just make the change here.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=130)** To make the footer fit for all smaller screen sizes, let's stack the footer text on top of the social media icons and center align it.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=138)** Let's switch over to the editor and add these adjustments.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=142)** In the CSS file, let's update the content-wrapper for the homepage.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=151)** We'll go to our home section and add a media query here, @media screen and our max-width.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=160)** We'll start with 650.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=165)** So this is where we'll adjust the padding in the wrapper and the content-bg to make space for the headings in the smaller screen sizes.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=174)** So I will just copy this selector here and add a padding of 10 pixels, and for the content-bg, I will take that down to 20 pixels.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=193)** Now for the footer, we'll use a media query with a min-width value of 860.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=203)** Since we're using min-width, this means 860 and larger.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=208)** So we'll move the flex styles to this media query.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=213)** That way, the footer content and social media lists will be aligned on opposite ends.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=218)** So all the flex related styles are these last three.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=225)** Copy it from here and add it to the media query.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=235)** For the smaller screen sizes, the footer isn't wide enough to align the content and social media lists on opposite ends.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=243)** So we'll just center align the content instead.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=246)** So now we'll need to add another media query for sizes that are smaller than 860.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=255)** So for this, we'll use a max-width value of 859.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=263)** We'll add our footer selector and then text-align set to center, and we'll add a little padding of 10 pixels so the content in the smaller screens don't line up right to the edge.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=280)** We'll save the file and check these changes in the browser.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=287)** So here we are at 865 pixels wide.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=292)** Now let's see how it looks.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=293)** So just under 860, the footer has changed to be center aligned, and let's see how it looks as we get smaller.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=302)** There's the other shift.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=304)** We have more space around the content-wrapper now, and one more shift with the font size changing, and we'll get down to 360 pixels.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=319)** Depending on your content and font choices, you may find that you'll need to make revisions here and there that are different than mine.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=326)** Our page layout wasn't too complex, but we also created a fluid foundation.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=331)** So oftentimes, if your layout is flexible to begin with, many of the responsive changes are just these little fixes.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=339)** We've come to the end of the course and the project exercise, but it doesn't have to end here.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=344)** Now that you have some tools under your belt, you can continue to experiment and customize your projects.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=350)** For example, here's a challenge.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=352)** On the homepage, it would be best to remove this large background image since you can't see much of it anyway, so it may not be worth the page load to add this large image to a small phone, or your option is to replace it with a smaller image that fits the space better and is smaller in file size.
>
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/project-media-queries-part-3?u=76281980&t=369)** It doesn't hurt to even start from the beginning and see what you can build on your own now that you've seen the bigger picture.

#### [Continuing beyond the essentials with CSS](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/continuing-beyond-the-essentials-with-css?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/continuing-beyond-the-essentials-with-css?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've covered a lot in this course, but mainly focused on the foundations of CSS and how to get started.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/continuing-beyond-the-essentials-with-css?u=76281980&t=7)** If you want to know where to go next, there are more intermediate and advanced topics to explore such as CSS animations, using icon fonts, and even a deeper dive into the topics we did cover, such as grid, flexbox, and other less commonly used properties and more advanced selectors.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/continuing-beyond-the-essentials-with-css?u=76281980&t=25)** You can even try to build out the project again from scratch or check out some of our other CSS courses in the library.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/continuing-beyond-the-essentials-with-css?u=76281980&t=32)** Learning CSS is just like learning any other type of language.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/continuing-beyond-the-essentials-with-css?u=76281980&t=36)** It's going to take more than a few hours to become fluent.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/css-essential-training-2023/continuing-beyond-the-essentials-with-css?u=76281980&t=39)** Thanks for watching the course.
